HD      EU to hit elevator cartel with record billion-euro fine: sources
BY      DOM
WC     311 words
PD      16 February 2007
ET       04:54 PM
SN      Agence France Presse
SC      AFPR
LA       English
CY      Copyright Agence France-Presse, 2007 All reproduction and presentation rights reserved.

LP
BRUSSELS, Feb 16, 2007 (AFP) -
EU regulators will next week slap a record  fine of up to a billion euros (1.3 billion dollars) on four big
European and US makers of lifts for running an illegal  cartel, sources close to the matter said Friday.

TD
ThyssenKrupp of Germany,  Otis of the United States,  Schindler of Switzerland and  Kone of Finland will be targeted by the EU competition watchdog as a result of a probe it has been conducting for several years, the sources said.

EU Competition Commissioner Neelie Kroes, Europe's top antitrust regulator, is leading a campaign against illegal cartels, which she says undermine competition and drive up prices for consumers.

The fine to be imposed on the lift makers will be "between 900 million and one billion euros," according to the sources, which will make it the biggest penalty ever imposed by the regulators at the European Commission.

EU investigators uncovered evidence that the companies operated a  cartel for between five and 10 years around the turn of the century by sharing confidential commercial information and secretly agreeing on prices.

The European Union's executive arm is scheduled to confirm the decision on Wednesday, one of the sources said. But the  commission's agenda could be changed at the last minute.
A European  Commission spokesman declined to comment on the matter.
After bumper  fines in 2006, the EU regulators in January ordered 10 companies to pay 750 million euros
(984 million dollars) for fixing prices and carving up the market in electrical generation equipment.

That fine was the second biggest EU regulators have imposed in a  cartel case after slapping 855 million euros in penalties on eight vitamin makers in 2001. It was later reduced to 790 million euros by an EU court.

HD      EU to hit elevator cartel with record billion-euro fine: sources
BY      DOM
WC     498 words
PD      16 February 2007
ET      06:20 PM
SN      Agence France Presse
SC      AFPR
LA       English
CY      Copyright Agence France-Presse, 2007 All reproduction and presentation rights reserved.

LP
BRUSSELS, Feb 16, 2007 (AFP) -
EU regulators, who have vowed to clamp down on illegal cartels, are poised to slap a record billion-euro
fine on Europe's four biggest lift makers for fixing prices, sources said Friday.

TD
ThyssenKrupp of Germany,  Otis of the United States,  Schindler of Switzerland and  Kone of Finland will be targeted by the EU competition watchdog as a result of a probe it has been conducting for several years, sources close to the matter told AFP.

EU Competition Commissioner Neelie Kroes, Europe's top antitrust regulator, is leading a campaign against illegal cartels, which she says undermine competition and drive up prices for consumers.

The fine to be imposed on the lift makers will be "between 900 million and one billion euros," (1.2-1.3 billion dollars) according to the sources, which will make it the biggest penalty ever imposed by the regulators at the European  Commission.

Under EU antitrust rules,  fines on companies participating in a  cartel can run as high as 10 percent of their global revenues, although such a heavy penalty has so for never been imposed on a group.

EU investigators uncovered evidence that the four lift makers operated a  cartel for between five and 10 years around the turn of the century by sharing confidential commercial information and secretly agreeing on prices.

The illegal activity took place in Germany, the biggest lift market in Europe, as well as Belgium, Luxembourg and the Netherlands.

The European Union's executive arm is scheduled to confirm the decision on Wednesday, one of the sources said. But the  commission's agenda could be changed at the last minute.
A European  Commission spokesman declined to comment on the matter.

Kone of Finland also had no comment.

After bumper  fines in 2006, the EU regulators in January ordered 10 companies to pay 750 million euros for fixing prices and carving up the market in electrical generation equipment.

That fine was the second biggest EU regulators have imposed in a cartel case after slapping 855 million euros in penalties on eight vitamin makers in 2001. The vitamin makers' fine was later reduced to 790 million euros by an EU court.

After busting the power equipment club, Kroes warned that she "will not tolerate cartels in Europe and the damage that they do in any area of business."
Despite the record the lift  fine will set, higher penalties are likely in future cases after the  commission
tightened its rules for calculating  fines, making them heavier for repeat offenders and long-lasting cartels.


Page 5 of 169 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
The reform could potentially cost guilty companies up to 10 times more than before.

The commission was also given a boost in its anti-cartel campaign earlier this month with a ruling from the European Court of Justice which gave regulators more freedom to fix  fines, especially how far back regulators can take previous offences into account.

BRUSSELS, Feb 16, 2007 (AFP) -

EU regulators are poised to come down hard next week on a  cartel of lift makers with a record  fine of close to one billion euros as part of a clampdown on the illegal clubs, sources said Friday.

TD
ThyssenKrupp of Germany,  Otis of the United States,  Schindler of Switzerland and  Kone of Finland will be targeted by the EU competition watchdog as a result of a probe it has been conducting for several years, sources close to the matter told AFP.

EU Competition Commissioner Neelie Kroes, Europe's top antitrust regulator, is leading a campaign against illegal cartels, which she says undermine competition and drive up prices for consumers.

The fine to be imposed on the lift makers will be "between 900 million and one billion euros," (1.2-1.3 billion dollars) according to the sources, which will make it the biggest penalty ever imposed by the regulators at the European  Commission.

Under EU antitrust rules,  fines on companies participating in a  cartel can run as high as 10 percent of their global revenues, although such a heavy penalty has so for never been imposed on a group.

EU investigators uncovered evidence that the four lift makers operated a  cartel for between five and 10 years around the turn of the century by sharing confidential commercial information and secretly agreeing on prices.

The illegal activity took place in Germany, the biggest lift market in Europe, as well as Belgium, Luxembourg and the Netherlands.

The European Union's executive arm is scheduled to confirm the decision on Wednesday, one of the sources said. But the  commission's agenda could be changed at the last minute.
A European  Commission spokesman declined to comment on the matter.

Kone also had no comment but did acknowledge that it has cooperated with regulators on the case since
2004.

After bumper  fines in 2006, EU regulators in January ordered 10 companies to pay 750 million euros for fixing prices and carving up the market in electrical generation equipment.

That fine was the second biggest EU regulators have imposed in a cartel case after slapping 855 million euros in penalties on eight vitamin makers in 2001. The vitamin makers' fine was later reduced to 790 million euros by an EU court.

After busting the power equipment club, Kroes warned that she "will not tolerate cartels in Europe and the damage that they do in any area of business."
Despite the record the lift  fine will set, higher penalties are likely in future cases after the  commission
tightened its rules for calculating  fines, making them heavier for repeat offenders and long-lasting cartels.

The reform could potentially cost guilty companies up to 10 times more than before.

The commission was also given a boost in its anti-cartel campaign earlier this month with a ruling from the European Court of Justice that gave regulators more freedom to fix  fines, especially how far back regulators can take previous offences into account.

BRUSSELS (afx) - The European  Commission will impose a record fine of almost 1 bln eur on four leading elevator manufacturers on Wednesday for alleged  cartel activity, sources close to the dossier told Agence France-Presse.

The commission is likely to issue a ruling on Feb 21 in the long-running case regarding an alleged  cartel among leading elevator manufacturers including ThyssenKrupp AG,  Schindler Group, Kone Corp and United Technologies Corp's  Otis Elevators  Co.

TD
The Commission began formal proceedings on the matter in Oct 2005.

LONDON (Dow Jones)--The world's biggest lift manufacturers are bracing themselves for a record fine from the European  Commission that could amount to almost GBP673 million for alleged price-fixing activities, the Times reports Saturday, citing industry sources.

The paper says that according to industry sources, some evidence of  cartel-like behavior had been investigated in German and the Benelux countries, but insisted that this didn't amount to Europe-wide price-fixing.

TD
Complaints from smaller rivals are believed to have led to the launch of an inquiry nearly three years ago into the so-called "big-four" lift manufacturers: United Technologies Corp's  Otis Elevators,  based in the U.S. and Paris;  Schindler Group of Switzerland; ThyssenKrupp (TKA.XE) of Germany and the Helsinki-based Kone Group (KNEBV.HE).

LONDON, Feb 17, 2007 (DJCS via Comtex) --

The world's biggest lift manufacturers are bracing themselves for a record fine from the European Commission that could amount to almost GBP673 million for alleged price-fixing activities, the Times reports Saturday, citing industry sources.

TD

Complaints from smaller rivals are believed to have led to the launch of an inquiry nearly three years ago into the so-called "big-four" lift manufacturers: United Technologies Corp's  Otis Elevators,  based in the U.S. and Paris;  Schindler Group of Switzerland; ThyssenKrupp (TKA.XE) of Germany and the Helsinki-based Kone Group (KNEBV.HE).

SE           Business
HD           Big four lift makers face Euro 1bn EU fine in cartel case
BY           David Charter, Brussels
WC          375 words
PD           17 February 2007
SN           The Times
SC           T
LA            English
CY           © 2007 Times Newspapers Ltd. All rights reserved

LP
The word's biggest lift manufacturers are bracing themselves for a record  fine from the European Commission that could amount to almost Euro 1 billion (Pounds 673 million) for alleged  price-fixing activities.

Industry sources told The Times that some evidence of  cartel-like behaviour had been investigated in Germany and the Benelux countries (Belgium, the Netherlands and Luxembourg), but insisted that this did not amount to Europe-wide  price-fixing.

TD
Complaints from smaller rivals are believed to have led to the launch of an inquiry nearly three years ago into the so-called "big four" lift manufacturers: United Technologies Corp's (UTC)  Otis Elevators, based in the United States and Paris;  Schindler Group of Switzerland; ThyssenKrupp from Germany; and the Helsinki-based  Kone Group.

In March 2004, UTC said that an internal investigation had given it "reason to believe that some  Otis employees in a small number of locations may have engaged in activities in violation of  Otis and UTC policies and competition law".

The Commission has the power to  fine companies up to 10 per cent of turnover for  cartel behaviour. The four big lift companies account for about half of the global $37 billion (Pounds 19 billion)  elevator industry between them.

The largest set of  fines yet imposed on a cartel came in January, when the  Commission fined 11 groups of companies Euro 750 million for participating in a  cartel for gas-insulated switchgear products between 1988 and 2004.
A new record fine is likely to be announced against the lift companies on Wednesday, according to
Agence France Presse yesterday. The  Commission refused to comment.
If they do receive such a big  fine, the companies are likely to appeal against the amount.

Neelie Kroes, the EU's Competition Commissioner, has given notice that she will not shy away from using the full extent of her powers to break cartels in Europe.

The vast elevator market in Europe has only just been overtaken in volume of new sales by China, which pipped annual European sales of 120,000 lifts. But Europe has the largest stock of existing elevators,  beating even the US.
(c) Times Newspapers Ltd, 2007

HD        UPDATE 1-Kone shares slip on EU fine reports
WC        329 words
PD         19 February 2007
ET         09:21 AM
SN         Reuters News
SC         LBA
LA         English
CY         (c) 2007 Reuters Limited

LP
(Adds comments, updates share)

HELSINKI, Feb 19 (Reuters) - Shares in Finnish lift and escalator  maker Kone slipped on Monday after media reports that it and other lift-makers could receive EU fines totalling 1 billion euros ($1.31 billion)
in a cartel probe.

TD
Kone shares traded 1.3 percent lower at 46.31 euros by 0920 GMT after opening down 4.1 percent, against a 0.3 percent gain in the Helsinki bourse general index <.OMXHPI>.

Reuters reported on Thursday that the European  Commission is expected to  fine several companies, including Kone, U.S.-based  Otis, which is owned by United Technologies , ThyssenKrupp of Germany and Schindler of Switzerland, according to a source familiar with the case.

The source said the fines would be imposed at a  Commission meeting on Wednesday, but did not say which firm would be fined or by how much.
Reports in Finnish media said the  fine could be 1 billion euros in total.

If a fine of that size were to be divided according to sales,  Kone could be penalised by up to 180 million euros ($237 million), analysts at FIM Securities said in a note to clients.

Kone spokeswoman Minna Mars said the company had received no new information from the Commission and could not comment on the possible  fine. She also said  Kone had not been told when the decision would be made public.

Sampo Brisk, analyst at OKO Bank, said market worries were less about the size of the  fine, and more about the impact of the ruling on the industry as a whole.

"Cartel investigations are unfortunate mainly for image.  Kone's balance sheet is so strong that even if it got a large fine, it will not impact the valuation much," said Brisk, who has an "accumulate" recommendation on the stock.

SE           Business Report
HD           Lift makers face stiff EU price-fixing fine
BY           Pat.Lucas
WC          294 words
PD           19 February 2007
SN           The Star SC           THESTR LA           English
CY           © Copyright 2007 Independent Newspapers (UK) Limited. All rights reserved.

LP
New York – EU regulators plan to  fine four of the biggest lift makers at least E900 million (R8.5 billion)
for price-fixing, according to a person with knowledge of the penalty.

United Technologies’  Otis division, the world’s largest lift maker,  Schindler Holding, ThyssenKrupp and Kone would be penalised, said the person, who declined to be identified because the decision was not public. About half of the  fine would be against ThyssenKrupp, the person said.

TD
The fine, to be announced as early as Wednesday, would be the largest by the European
Commission, exceeding a E790.5 million penalty against a vitamin  cartel in 2001.

European competition commissioner Neelie Kroes has made busting cartels her priority since taking over from Mario Monti in November 2004.

Christian Obst, an analyst at HVB in Munich, said: “Nobody expected such a high  fine. It will have an effect on the companies. The message is there needs to be more competition in the market.”

The commission alleges a price-fixing cartel took place in Belgium, Germany, Luxembourg and the Netherlands between 1998 and 2004. Jonathan Todd, a  commission spokesperson, declined to comment.

The EU regulator would  fine the four companies as much as E1 billion, AFP reported on Friday, citing people close to the matter.

“This could affect the future profitability of  elevator companies, which have historically had high margins and charged high prices,” Obst said. He estimated that the lift market this year would generate profit of at least E3 billion.

“These are just rumours,” said a spokesperson for  Kone. A spokesperson for  Schindler said it did not comment on speculation. All four companies said they had been co-operating with the regulator. – Bloomberg

BRUSSELS (Dow Jones)--The European  Commission on Wednesday is likely to  fine four elevator
and escalator  makers for fixing prices, according to a person familiar with the matter.

The commission, the European Union's regulatory arm, two years ago charged United Technologies Corp.'s (UTX) Otis unit, ThyssenKrupp AG (TKA.XE),  Schindler Holding AG (SCHN.EB) and  Kone OYJ (KNEBV.HE) with operating cartels in Belgium, Germany, the Netherlands and Luxembourg between 1995 and 2004.

TD
The commission declined to comment on the details of the case or to say whether a decision to punish the manufacturers was scheduled for Wednesday.

The regulator likely will  fine the companies a total of EUR900 million to EUR1 billion, according to an Agence France Presse report that cited unnamed sources. That total would surpass the  commission's highest-ever cartel penalty, a EUR750 million  fine levied last month against 11 companies that fixed prices for power-grid components.

Under new E.U. antitrust guidelines established last year, the commission can now levy larger fines and apply an automatic 50% increase for repeat  cartel offenders.

HD      CARTELS : LIFT MAKERS FACE RECORD FINE WC     295 words
PD      20 February 2007
SN      Europolitics SC      EURREP LA     English
CY      Copyright 2007 Europe Information Service All Rights Reserved

LP
The European  Commission is set to impose a record  fine on four lift makers for operating an illegal cartel. Penalties of up to €900 million are expected to be announced on 21 February against ThyssenKrupp of Germany,  Otis of the United States, Switzerland's  Schindler and Finnish company Krone. The biggest  fine in this international price-fixing ring is thought to be pending against ThyssenKrupp, the world's largest producer of elevators.  Reports allege that between 1998 and 2004, the companies made secret agreements to fix prices and share confidential information in Belgium, Germany, Luxembourg and the Netherlands.

TD
Only a month ago, the Commission imposed a staggering €750 million  fine on 11 companies for involvement in a cartel in the gas insulated switchgear sector (see Europolitics 3233). A €900 million  fine would be the highest ever imposed by the  Commission, beating even the €855 million imposed in the huge vitamin cartel in 2001. This shows that Competition Commissioner Neelie Kroes indeed means business when she talks of a zero-tolerance policy against cartels - illegal conspiracies which damage business and consumers alike.

The discovery of these major cartels vindicates the  Commission's 2004 decision to turn some of its powers concerning anti-competitive agreements over to national authorities. The rationale at the time was that it would free up time and resources within the  Commission to combat large-scale cartel activity: the strategy appears to be paying off.  Fines are likely to continue to rise when the Commission's new fining guidelines kick in. These will increase the overall level of  fines from the current 10% of the total annual turnover to 30% of annual sales relating to the  cartel. They also allow even larger  fines for repeat offenders.

HD         EU To Impose 1.0 Bln Euro Fine on Lift Makers - Report
WC        250 words
PD         20 February 2007
SN         German News Digest
SC         GERMND
LA         English
CY         (c) 2007 AII Data Processing Ltd. All Rights Reserved.

LP
The European Union will impose a  fine for up to 1.0 bln euro ($1.317 bln) on four lift makers, among which is German steel and engineering group ThyssenKrupp AG, German daily Handelsblatt reported on February 20, 2007, citing industry sources.

The fine, which is the highest  cartel fine ever imposed by the EU, will be between 900 mln euro ($1.186 bln) and 1.0 bln euro ($1.317 bln). Besides ThyssenKrupp, it will also affect U.S. United Technologies Corp's  Otis Elevators  Co Ltd, Switzerland's  Schindler and Finland's  Kone Corporation.

TD
A spokesperson for the European  Commission (EC) did not comment on the total size of the  fine. The
EC is expected to announce its decision on February 21, 2007.

The four companies are alleged to have divided between themselves orders and tenders for  elevators and escalators in Germany, Belgium, Luxembourg and the Netherlands for the last ten years, according to branch experts.
ThyssenKrupp will account for around half of the  fine, as it has repeatedly trespassed anti-cartel
regulations.

Industry sources said the  cartel agreements must have applied only within the actual country borders and varied from country to country. The  price-fixing agreements in Germany were only valid for escalators. The EU competition authorities carried out separate investigations in the different markets. The fine, however, will refer to the delinquency in all countries.

In all four countries, high-ranking managers held regular meetings related to the  cartel. The commission found evidence that the companies knew their behavior was illegal. The managers were careful to avoid detection, meeting in rural areas or abroad and using prepaid mobile phone cards to avoid tracking.

TD
None of the companies qualified for the full immunity usually given to whistle-blowers.  Kone's penalty was reduced by about EUR138 million because it was the first to disclose the cartels in Belgium and Luxembourg and provided key evidence in the German investigation. A key question in this case will be whether the companies' customers decide to sue. Over the past few years, the commission has encouraged private damages claims to further deter  cartel offenders.
"Companies have the option of seeking damages" or renegotiating their contracts, said the
commission's antitrust spokesman Jonathan Todd.

HD         UPDATE: EU Fines Elevator Makers EUR992M For Price-Fixing
WC        666 words
PD         21 February 2007
ET         05:31 PM
SN         Dow Jones Asian Equities Report
SC         AER
LA          English
CY         (c) 2007 Dow Jones & Company, Inc.

LP
(Updates an item, published from around 1125 GMT, with company responses and further details.)

BRUSSELS (Dow Jones)--The European Commission on Wednesday levied Europe's largest-ever antitrust penalty, fining five  elevator and escalator  makers a total of EUR992 million for fixing prices.

TD
ThyssenKrupp AG (TKA.XE) was fined EUR479.7 million, United Technologies Corp.'s (UTX)  Otis unit was fined EUR224.9 million, Schindler Holding AG (SCHN.EB) was hit with a EUR143.7 million penalty and Kone was fined EUR142.1 million.

The sum of these  fines, which topped a EUR790 million penalty levied in 2001 against makers of vitamin products, reflects the  Commission's expanded powers to crack down on corporate cartels.

Under new antitrust guidelines established last year, the  Commission can levy larger fines and apply an automatic 50% increase for repeat  cartel offenders. Since ThyssenKrupp had a previous  cartel punishment - a EUR3.2 million  fine in 1998 for fixing stainless steel prices - its  elevator penalty
included this added charge, bringing its  fine close to the record EUR497 million Microsoft Corp. (MSFT)
paid in 2004 for breaking monopoly laws.

"Three million euros is lunch money," said Julian Joshua, a lawyer at the Howrey LLP law firm in Brussels and a former deputy head of the  commission's cartel team. Steeper  fines, such as the ones in the elevator case, "can have important financial consequences for companies," he added.

ThyssenKrupp shares fell about 2% to EUR38.60 after the  fine was disclosed. The other three companies had smaller dips in their share prices.
The Commission launched its investigation into the  elevatorand escalator  businesses in January
2004. Its probe showed that the four companies fixed prices and rigged bids in Belgium, Germany, the
Netherlands and Luxembourg between 1995 and 2004.

The Commission said Mitsubishi Elevator Europe BV, a division of Mitsubishi Electric Corp. (6503.TO), participated in the Dutch cartel and fined the company EUR1.8 million.

"It is outrageous that the construction and maintenance costs of buildings, including hospitals, have been artificially bloated by these cartels. The national management of these companies knew what they were doing was wrong, but they tried to conceal their action and went ahead anyway," the Commission's top antitrust official Neelie Kroes said in a statement.

In all four countries, high-ranking managers held regular meetings related to the  cartel. The commission found evidence that the companies knew their behavior was illegal. The managers were careful to avoid detection, meeting in rural areas or abroad and using prepaid mobile phone cards to avoid tracking. The  Commission said none of the companies contested these facts or other evidence unearthed in the investigation.

None of the companies qualified for the full immunity usually given to whistle-blowers.  Kone's penalty was reduced by about EUR138 million because it was the first to disclose the cartels in Belgium and Luxembourg and provided key evidence in the German investigation.  Otis' penalty was reduced by over EUR208 million because it disclosed the Dutch  cartel and cooperated with investigations in the other countries.

Page 25 of 169 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
Otis said it will appeal the  Commission's decision at the European Court of First Instance in Luxembourg. Its parent company United Technologies said the  fine "will have no material impact" on its financial condition.

Schindler said it was "very surprised" at the size of the  fine since the companies were charged with local collusion, not running a pan-European  cartel.

Finland-based Kone and Germany's ThyssenKrupp said they will examine the  Commission's decision before deciding whether to appeal.

A key question in this case will be whether the companies' customers decide to sue. Over the past few years, the Commission has encouraged private damages claims to further deter  cartel offenders.
"Companies have the option of seeking damages" or renegotiating their contracts, said the
Commission's antitrust spokesman Jonathan Todd.
-By Adam Cohen, Dow Jones Newswires; +322 741 1486; adam.cohen@dowjones.com [ 21-02-07
1631GMT ]

BRUSSELS, Feb 21, 2007 (AFP) -

EU competition regulators imposed on Wednesday a record fine of nearly a billion euros (1.3 billion dollars) on lift makers  Otis, Schindler, ThyssenKrupp and Kone for running an illegal  cartel.

TD
The European Commission accused the companies of operating a  cartel for the installation and maintenance of lifts and escalators in Belgium, Germany, Luxembourg and The Netherlands between at least 1995 and 2004.

The European Union's antitrust watchdog said it was fining them a total of 992 million euros between them for rigging bids for procurement contracts, fixing prices and sharing out projects among each other, carving up markets and exchanging sensitive information.

HD      EU brings  down lift cartel with record near billion-euro fine
BY      LT
WC     396 words
PD      21 February 2007
ET       01:17 PM
SN      Agence France Presse
SC      AFPR
LA       English
CY      Copyright Agence France-Presse, 2007 All reproduction and presentation rights reserved.

BRUSSELS, Feb 21, 2007 (AFP) -

EU competition regulators imposed on Wednesday a record fine of nearly a billion euros (1.3 billion dollars) on lift makers  Otis, Schindler, ThyssenKrupp and Kone for running an illegal  cartel.

TD
The European Commission accused the companies of operating a  cartel for the installation and maintenance of lifts and escalators in Belgium, Germany, Luxembourg and The Netherlands between at least 1995 and 2004.

The European Union's antitrust watchdog said it was fining them a combined 992 million euros for rigging bids for procurement contracts, fixing prices, sharing out projects, carving up markets and exchanging sensitive information.

The total fine also included a 1.8-million-euro penalty against Japanese company Mitsubishi for a role in the cartel limited to The Netherlands.

"It is outrageous that the construction and maintenance costs of buildings, including hospitals, have been artificially bloated by these cartels," Competition Commissioner Neelie Kroes stormed.

"The national management of these companies knew what they were doing was wrong, but they tried to conceal their action and went ahead anyway.

"The damage caused by this  cartel will last for many years because it covered not only the initial supply but also the subsequent maintenance of lifts and  elevators  -- for these companies the memory of this fine should last just as long," she added.

ThyssenKrupp was handed the heaviest  fine ever imposed on a single company in  cartel case because it was a repeat offender. The German group was  fined 480 million euros.

Otis, a unit of US conglomerate United Technologies Corporation, was given a  fine of 225 million euros for its role, followed by  Schindler with a penalty of 144 million euros and  Kone with 142 million euros.

Kroes, Europe's top antitrust regulator, is leading a campaign against illegal cartels and has been chalking up record fines against companies targeted by her crackdown.

After bumper  fines in 2006, EU regulators in January ordered 10 companies to pay 750 million euros for fixing prices and carving up the market in electrical generation equipment.

That fine was the second biggest EU regulators have imposed in a cartel case after slapping 855 million euros in penalties on eight vitamin makers in 2001. The vitamin makers' fine was later reduced to 790 million euros by an EU court.

HD      EU brings  down lift cartel with record near billion-euro fine

BRUSSELS, Feb 21, 2007 (AFP) -
EU competition regulators imposed their biggest  fine ever on Wednesday, hitting lift makers Otis,
Schindler, ThyssenKrupp and  Kone with a penalty of nearly a billion euros for running an illegal cartel.

TD
The European Commission accused the companies of operating a  cartel for the installation and maintenance of lifts and escalators in Belgium, Germany, Luxembourg and The Netherlands between at least 1995 and 2004.

The European Union's antitrust watchdog said it was fining them a combined 992 million euros (1.3 billion dollars) for rigging bids for procurement contracts, fixing prices, sharing out projects, carving up markets and exchanging sensitive information.

The total fine also included a 1.8-million-euro penalty against Japanese company Mitsubishi for a role in the cartel limited to The Netherlands.

"It is outrageous that the construction and maintenance costs of buildings, including hospitals, have been artificially bloated by these cartels," Competition Commissioner Neelie Kroes stormed.

"The national management of these companies knew what they were doing was wrong, but they tried to conceal their action and went ahead anyway.

"The damage caused by this  cartel will last for many years because it covered not only the initial supply but also the subsequent maintenance of lifts and  elevators  -- for these companies the memory of this fine should last just as long," she added.
ThyssenKrupp was handed the heaviest  fine ever imposed by EU regulators on a single company in a
cartel case because it was a repeated offender. The German group was  fined 480 million euros.

Otis, a unit of US conglomerate United Technologies Corporation, was given a  fine of 225 million euros for its role, followed by  Schindler with a penalty of 144 million euros and  Kone with 142 million euros.
EU antitrust investigators conducted so-called "dawn raids" on lift and  escalator  makers across Europe in
January 2004 after getting a tip off about the  cartel.
After the raids, the companies handed over more evidence in the hope of winning leniency or reduced
fines.

The European  Commission said that high-ranking national-level management ran the  cartel by holding secret meetings in bars and restaurants. They also visited the countryside and travelled abroad and used pre-paid mobile phone cards to avoid regulators' scrutiny.

Kroes, Europe's top antitrust regulator, is leading a campaign against illegal cartels and has been chalking up record fines against companies targeted by her crackdown.



Page 31 of 169 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
After bumper  fines in 2006, EU regulators in January ordered 10 companies to pay 750 million euros for fixing prices and carving up the market in electrical generation equipment.
After the lift makers case, that  fine is now the third biggest ever against a  cartel.

Until Wednesday, the biggest  fine in a European cartel case was 855 million euros in penalties imposed on eight vitamin makers in 2001. The vitamin makers' fine was later reduced to 790 million euros by an EU court.

Despite the record  fine on the lift makers, higher penalties are likely in future cases after the commission tightened its rules for calculating  fines, making them heavier for repeat offenders and long-lasting cartels.
The reform could potentially cost guilty companies up to 10 times more than before.

The commission was also given a boost in its anti-cartel campaign earlier this month with a ruling from the European Court of Justice that gave regulators more freedom to fix  fines, especially how far back regulators can take previous offences into account.

WASHINGTON, Feb 21, 2007 (AFP) -
The US parent company of elevator maker Otis said Wednesday it would appeal against a record EU
antitrust  fine imposed against a lift cartel.

TD
United Technologies Corp. (UTC) said it had "cooperated fully" with the European Union's executive
commission during the investigation into the market-rigging  cartel.
"Otis will appeal the  commission's decision to the European Court of the First Instance," a UTC
statement said.

"We are disappointed by the conduct revealed through the  commission's and UTC's own investigations," UTC chief executive George David said.
"UTC will not tolerate unethical behaviors of any kind," he said, noting that nine  Otis employees in
Europe had been "quickly identified and dismissed" for violating the company's code of ethics.

The statement added: "The outcome of the case will have no material impact on UTC's financial condition and no material impact on  Otis' competitive position."

The European Commission ruled that the companies ran a  cartel for the installation and maintenance of lifts and escalators in Belgium, Germany, Luxembourg and The Netherlands between at least 1995 and
2004.
The combined fine came to a record 992 million euros (1.3 billion dollars).
ThyssenKrupp was handed the heaviest  fine ever imposed by EU regulators on a single company in a
cartel case because it was a repeat offender. The German group was  fined 480 million euros.

Otis was given a fine of 225 million euros for its role, followed by  Schindler with a penalty of 144 million euros and Kone with 142 million euros.

HD      EU brings  down lift cartel with record billion-euro fine

BRUSSELS, Feb 21, 2007 (AFP) -
EU competition regulators imposed their biggest  fine ever on Wednesday, hitting lift makers Otis,
Schindler, ThyssenKrupp and  Kone with a penalty of nearly a billion euros for running an illegal cartel.

TD
"The result of this  cartel is that taxpayers, public authorities and property developers have been ripped off big time," said the competition commissioner's spokesman.

The European  Commission, the EU's antitrust watchdog, accused the companies of operating a  cartel for the installation and maintenance of lifts and  escalators in Belgium, Germany, Luxembourg and The Netherlands between at least 1995 and 2004.

It said it was fining them a combined 992 million euros (1.3 billion dollars) for rigging bids for procurement contracts, fixing prices, sharing out projects, carving up markets and exchanging sensitive information.

The total fine also included a 1.8-million-euro penalty against Japanese company Mitsubishi for a role in the cartel limited to The Netherlands.

Competition Commissioner Neelie Kroes, Europe's top antitrust regulator, is leading a campaign against illegal cartels and has been chalking up record  fines against companies targeted by her crackdown.

"It is outrageous that the construction and maintenance costs of buildings, including hospitals, have been artificially bloated by these cartels," Kroes stormed on Wednesday after the record  fine was announced.

"The national management of these companies knew what they were doing was wrong, but they tried to conceal their action and went ahead anyway," she said.

Her spokesman Jonathan Todd told journalists that the lift  cartel's effects "will be felt for the next 20 to 50 years because a lot of these companies make their money from the maintenance which is done following the installation of  escalators and elevators."

ThyssenKrupp was handed the heaviest  fine ever imposed by EU regulators on a single company in a cartel case because it was a repeated offender. The German group's  fine was increased by 50 percent to a total 480 million euros because of its history of participating in cartels.

Otis, a unit of US conglomerate United Technologies Corp., was given a  fine of 225 million euros for its role, followed by Switzerland-based  Schindler with a penalty of 144 million euros and  Kone of Finland with 142 million euros.

United Technologies said that  Otis would lodge an appeal with an EU court while ThyssenKrupp,  Kone and Schindler said separately they would examine the  commission's case against them before deciding whether to challenge it.

Schindler said in a statement that it was "very surprised at the size of the  fine since the European Commission found no evidence of pan-European collusion among companies in the European  elevator industry."

Page 35 of 169 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
EU antitrust investigators conducted so-called "dawn raids" on lift and  escalator  makers across Europe in
January 2004 after getting a tip-off about the  cartel.
After the raids, the companies handed over more evidence in the hope of winning leniency or reduced
fines.

The European  Commission said that high-ranking national-level management ran the  cartel by holding secret meetings in bars and restaurants. They also visited the countryside and travelled abroad and used pre-paid mobile phone cards to avoid regulators' scrutiny.

After bumper  fines in 2006, EU regulators in January ordered 10 companies to pay 750 million euros for fixing prices and carving up the market in electrical generation equipment.
After the lift makers case, that  fine is now the third biggest ever against a  cartel.

Until Wednesday, the biggest  fine in a European cartel case was 855 million euros in penalties imposed on eight vitamin makers in 2001. The vitamin makers' fine was later reduced to 790 million euros by an EU court.

Despite the record  fine on the lift makers, higher penalties are likely in future cases after the commission tightened its rules for calculating  fines, making them heavier for repeat offenders and long-lasting cartels.

HD      US shares mixed on inflation jitters
BY      BUR
WC     662 words
PD      21 February 2007
ET       11:12 PM
SN      Agence France Presse
SC      AFPR
LA       English
CY      Copyright Agence France-Presse, 2007 All reproduction and presentation rights reserved.

NEW YORK, Feb 21, 2007 (AFP) -

US shares ended mixed Wednesday after a surprise hike in consumer prices revived fears about the outlook for inflation and Federal Reserve policy.

TD
The Dow Jones Industrial Average closed down 48.23 points (0.38 percent) at 2,738.41, after notching up a record closing high of 12,786.64 Tuesday.

"The CPI and the BoJ raising interest rates weighed on the market today," Cowen and Co. analyst Michael Malone said after the Bank of Japan raised interest rates for only the second time in over six years.
But the Nasdaq composite shrugged off the inflation numbers to gain 5.38 points (0.21 percent) to
2,518.42. The broad-market Standard and Poor's 500 meanwhile fell 2.05 points (0.14 percent) to close
at 1,457.63.

The US consumer price index (CPI) jumped 0.2 percent last month compared to December as surging food and medical costs drove up headline inflation, the Labor Department said.
The "core" rate, which excludes volatile food and energy prices, went up a larger 0.3 percent in January. "The CPI news takes some of the wind out of the excessively optimistic 'Goldilocks' forecast of moderate
economic growth with low inflation," Briefing.com president Dick Green said.

"This one-month read on inflation does not signal a new trend, or even undermine the belief that inflation trends are moderate," he added.
"It does, however, take away some of the optimism that inflation has moderated to such an extent that the
Fed will be able to lower interest rates later this year."

Minutes from the Fed's January 31 meeting, released Wednesday, showed that central bankers were encouraged by easing price pressures but "did not yet see a downtrend in core inflation as definitively established."

Among stocks in focus, computer maker Hewlett-Packard slumped 4.75 percent to 41.08 dollars after issuing a disappointing earnings outlook late Tuesday, despite posting better-than-expected profits for the past quarter.

"Hewlett-Packard is really weighing on the Dow today. HP is accounting for 15 points of the downside of the Dow, so if you remove that, it would be very different," Malone said.

He added: "We're just slightly off. We had multi-year highs yesterday. I think it's contributing to today's pulback but I don't think it's anything that investors are really finding overly troubling."




Page 37 of 169 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
AG Edwards chief market strategist Al Goldman said Tuesday's record finish for the Dow had "demonstrated the underlying momentum and positive economic fundamentals supporting the stock market."

"However, the upside has been losing some of its previous oomph. This is normal action after so many up days and we will be watching for signs of increased fatigue," he said.

United Technologies Corp. closed down 0.81 percent at 67.58 after its elevator-making division Otis was ruled by the European Union to have formed part of a market-rigging cartel.
The EU's executive commission imposed a record antitrust fine of 992 million euros (1.3 billion dollars)
on cartel members Otis, Schindler, ThyssenKrupp and Kone.

Otis, whose share of the  fine was 225 million euros, said it would appeal to an EU court.

JetBlue Airways recovered 2.25 percent to 13.19 dollars, a day after slumping 5.0 percent, following its release of a "customer bill of rights" to compensate passengers hit by last-minute cancellations.

The budget US airline has been in a storm of controversy after it cancelled nearly 1,100 flights last week owing to heavy snow, stranding thousands of passengers.
Bond prices, which move in opposite directions to bond yields, weakened on the inflation news.

The yield on the 10-year US Treasury bond rose to 4.692 percent from 4.680 percent Tuesday, while that on the 30-year bond advanced to 4.789 percent from 4.780.

HD     OUTLOOK Thyssenkrup, Otis, Schindler, Kone braced for 1 bln eur EU cartel fine
WC    997 words
PD     21 February 2007
SN     AFX Asia SC     AFXASI LA      English
CY     (c) 2007, AFX Asia. All rights reserved.

LP
BRUSSELS (AFX) - Four of the world's biggest lift makers, ThyssenKrupp AG, United Technologies Corp's Otis Elevators  Co Ltd, Schindler AG and Kone Oyj, are bracing themselves for heavy sanctions set to be imposed tomorrow by the European Commission totalling close to 1 bln eur for alleged  cartel behaviour beginning nine years ago, sources said.

Sources told Agence France-Presse that said the collective  cartel fine imposed on the German, US, Swiss and Finnish manufacturers would set a record for EU antitrust regulators. Reports also said that the majority of the  fine would be levied at ThyssenKrupp.

TD
The sanction could come as early as tomorrow, with European Commissioners, including EU competition commissioner Neelie Kroes, meeting to discuss the timing and  fine detail of the decision on the
long-standing case.
Industry sources told DPA News Agency that the  commission is to rubber-stamp its ruling tomorrow. The
commission has declined to comment.

ThyssenKrupp said it was cooperating with the body, but a company spokeswoman declined to provide further details on the matter.

The commission will say that the companies engaged in  price-fixing and market collusion in Belgium, Germany, Luxembourg and the Netherlands between 1998 and 2004.
In Jan 2004, EU executive officials raided the premises of the four groups after receiving  cartel
allegations from third parties with regard to the European  elevator and escalator  market.
Third parties complained that they were not supplied with spare parts by the members of the supposed
cartel.

The commission also conducted further raids in Belgium, Luxembourg and Germany in early March of that year.

United Technologies confirmed at the time that a "small number" of employees at the European operations of its  Otis elevator unit may have violated company policy and competition rules.

Kone added that it found "local" anti-competitive behaviour at its offices in Belgium, Luxembourg and Germany following an internal investigation triggered by the EU raids, but said it did not find "evidence or indications" of any European-wide anti-competitive practices.

In a statement at the time,  Kone said it took "immediate measures to stop anything that could be considered as anti-competitive behaviour in the European Union." The company declined to specify the nature of the measures.

The commission finally issued formal charges to the companies -- set out as a 'statement of objections' -- in mid-October 2005.
The companies can appeal  fines at the EU's highest court, the European Court of Justice (ECJ).



Page 39 of 169 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
The fine is set to be the heaviest imposed by EU regulators after they levied 855.2 mln eur -- reduced on appeal to 790.5 mln -- in Nov 2001 against eight vitamin companies suspected of  cartel behaviour lasting nine years.
The companies implicated were Roche Holding AG, BASF AG, Aventis SA, Takeda, Solvay
Pharmaceuticals NV, Merck KGaA, Daiichi Pharmaceutical Co Ltd and Eisai Co Ltd.
Late last month, the  commission also fined 11 companies a total of 750.7 mln eur for participation in a
cartel for gas insulated switchgear projects between 1988 and 2004.
The companies involved were Siemens AG, ABB Ltd, Alstom, Areva, Fuji Co, Hitachi Japan AE Power
Systems, Mitsubishi Electric Corp, Schneider Electric, Toshiba Corp and VA Tech.

Kroes has made the fight against cartels one of the key policies of her tenure as head of the EU's competition department.

Under new guidelines set by the  commission in June last year, the  commission adopted new powers for its policing of antitrust abuses.

The commission will fix a basic fine of up to 30 pct of a company's sales of the product in the market where the abuse has taken place. The  fine will also be multiplied by the number of years during which a company participates in the infringement.
The overall ceiling for  fines remains 10 pct of global annual turnover.

In addition, a so-called 'entry fee' may be imposed, particularly on cartels, irrespective of the duration of the infringement. This is calculated as a sum equal to 15-25 pct of a company's annual sales, whatever the duration of the infringement.
The commission added that repeat offenders will also be  fined more than in the past.

The commission's previous practice has been to increase a  fine by 50 pct where the company has been found to have been previously involved in one or more similar infringements.

Under the new rules, the  commission can increase the fine up to 100 pct. It will also take into account not only its own previous decisions, but also those of EU member state national competition authorities.
Each prior infringement will justify an increase of the  fine.
In this regards, the  commission's remit to fight cartels was given a boost earlier this month after the ECJ
upheld a fine of 42.4 mln eur imposed by the EU's lower court on French food company Groupe Danone.

The court dismissed Danone's claim that sanctions imposed for repeated  cartel infringements have no basis in EU law.
A spokesman for Kroes said at the time that the ECJ for the first time "clearly confirmed the
commission's policy of increasing  fines for repeat offenders."

The changes do not affect the commission's rules on leniency, whereby  cartel whistleblowers can receive full immunity from fines. There are also graded  fine reductions for assistance in  cartel exposure.

There is no strict deadline to complete  cartel inquiries. Their duration depends on a number of factors, including; the complexity of each case, the extent to which the companies concerned co-operate, and the exercise of the rights of defence.

In Jan 2004, EU executive officials raided the premises of the four groups after receiving  cartel
allegations from third parties with regard to the European  elevator and escalator  market.
Third parties complained that they were not supplied with spare parts by the members of the supposed
cartel.

The commission also conducted further raids in Belgium, Luxembourg and Germany in early March of that year.

United Technologies confirmed at the time that a "small number" of employees at the European operations of its  Otis elevator unit may have violated company policy and competition rules.

Kone added that it found "local" anti-competitive behaviour at its offices in Belgium, Luxembourg and Germany following an internal investigation triggered by the EU raids, but said it did not find "evidence or indications" of any European-wide anti-competitive practices.

In a statement at the time,  Kone said it took "immediate measures to stop anything that could be considered as anti-competitive behaviour in the European Union." The company declined to specify the nature of the measures.

The commission finally issued formal charges to the companies -- set out as a 'statement of objections' -- in mid-October 2005.
The companies can appeal  fines at the EU's highest court, the European Court of Justice (ECJ).



Page 42 of 169 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
The fine is set to be the heaviest imposed by EU regulators after they levied 855.2 mln eur -- reduced on appeal to 790.5 mln -- in Nov 2001 against eight vitamin companies suspected of  cartel behaviour lasting nine years.
The companies implicated were Roche Holding AG, BASF AG, Aventis SA, Takeda, Solvay
Pharmaceuticals NV, Merck KGaA, Daiichi Pharmaceutical Co Ltd and Eisai Co Ltd.
Late last month, the  commission also fined 11 companies a total of 750.7 mln eur for participation in a
cartel for gas insulated switchgear projects between 1988 and 2004.
The companies involved were Siemens AG, ABB Ltd, Alstom, Areva, Fuji Co, Hitachi Japan AE Power
Systems, Mitsubishi Electric Corp, Schneider Electric, Toshiba Corp and VA Tech.

Kroes has made the fight against cartels one of the key policies of her tenure as head of the EU's competition department.

Under new guidelines set by the  commission in June last year, the  commission adopted new powers for its policing of antitrust abuses.

The commission will fix a basic fine of up to 30 pct of a company's sales of the product in the market where the abuse has taken place. The  fine will also be multiplied by the number of years during which a company participates in the infringement.
The overall ceiling for  fines remains 10 pct of global annual turnover.

In addition, a so-called 'entry fee' may be imposed, particularly on cartels, irrespective of the duration of the infringement. This is calculated as a sum equal to 15-25 pct of a company's annual sales, whatever the duration of the infringement.
The commission added that repeat offenders will also be  fined more than in the past.

The commission's previous practice has been to increase a  fine by 50 pct where the company has been found to have been previously involved in one or more similar infringements.

Under the new rules, the  commission can increase the fine up to 100 pct. It will also take into account not only its own previous decisions, but also those of EU member state national competition authorities.
Each prior infringement will justify an increase of the  fine.
In this regards, the  commission's remit to fight cartels was given a boost earlier this month after the ECJ
upheld a fine of 42.4 mln eur imposed by the EU's lower court on French food company Groupe Danone.

The court dismissed Danone's claim that sanctions imposed for repeated  cartel infringements have no basis in EU law.
A spokesman for Kroes said at the time that the ECJ for the first time "clearly confirmed the
commission's policy of increasing  fines for repeat offenders."

The changes do not affect the commission's rules on leniency, whereby  cartel whistleblowers can receive full immunity from fines. There are also graded  fine reductions for assistance in  cartel exposure.

There is no strict deadline to complete  cartel inquiries. Their duration depends on a number of factors, including; the complexity of each case, the extent to which the companies concerned co-operate, and the exercise of the rights of defence.

HD      EU fines ThyssenKrupp, Otis, Schindler, Kone 992 mln eur for lift cartel UPDATE WC     697 words
PD      21 February 2007
SN      AFX Asia SC      AFXASI LA      English
CY      (c) 2007, AFX Asia. All rights reserved.

LP
(Updates with further detail on  fines, cartel offence, EU  Commission comments)

BRUSSELS (AFX) - The European Commission said it has fined four of the world's biggest lift makers ThyssenKrupp AG, United Technologies Corp's Otis, Schindler AG and Kone Oyj a total of 992.3 mln eur for alleged cartel activity going back twelve years.

TD
The fine imposed is a record for the  commission, surpassing one of 855.2 mln eur -- reduced on appeal to 790.5 mln -- sanctioned against eight companies for operating a vitamin  cartel in Nov 2001.

The lion's share of the sanction was levied at ThyssenKrupp, which was  fined 479.7 mln eur, or 48.3 pct of the total  fine. The fines imposed on the group were increased by 50 pct, as it is a repeat offender.

Otis was fined 224.9 mln eur,  Schindler 143.7 mln, and  Kone 142.1 mln. The decision also implicates
17 units of the above groups.

In addition, Mitsubishi Elevator Europe BV was  fined a much smaller amount, 1.8 mln eur, for participating in the Dutch cartel.

Kone units received full immunity from  fines under the commission's leniency programme in respect of the cartels in Belgium and Luxembourg, as they were first to provide information about these cartels.
Similarly,  Otis Netherlands received full immunity in respect of the Netherlands  cartel.

The commission said the lift manufacturers engaged in  price-fixing and market collusion in Belgium, Germany, Luxembourg and the Netherlands between 1995 and 2004.

"These companies rigged bids for procurement contracts, fixed prices and allocated projects to each other, shared markets and exchanged commercially important and confidential information," the EU executive said.

"Business secrets and confidential information on bidding patterns and prices between the  cartel participants were also exchanged. Projects that were rigged included lifts and  escalators for hospitals, railway stations, shopping centres and commercial buildings".

The commission said that high-ranking national management -- such as managing directors, sales and services directors and heads of customer service departments -- participated in regular meetings and discussions.

It added that there is evidence that the companies were aware that their behaviour was illegal and took care to avoid detection.

"They usually met in bars and restaurants, they travelled to the countryside or even abroad, and they used pre-paid mobile phone cards to avoid tracking," it added.

"The effects of this  cartel may continue for twenty to fifty years as maintenance is often done by the companies that installed the equipment in the first place".
The commission said the allocation of projects was similar in all four member states


Page 47 of 169 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
In Jan 2004, EU executive officials raided the premises of the four groups after receiving  cartel
allegations from third parties with regard to the European  elevator and escalator  market.
The commission finally issued formal charges to the companies -- set out as a 'statement of objections'
-- in mid-October 2005.

In their responses to the  commission's charges, the EU executive said the companies did not contest the facts and none of them requested an oral hearing.
The companies can appeal the decision at the EU's highest court, the European Court of Justice. Under EU antitrust rules, the EU can  fine a company participating in a  cartel up to 10 pct of its global
revenues.

EU competition commissioner Neelie Kroes said: "It is outrageous that the construction and maintenance costs of buildings, including hospitals, have been artificially bloated by these cartels."

"The national management of these companies knew what they were doing was wrong, but they tried to conceal their action and went ahead anyway".

"The damage caused by this  cartel will last for many years because it covered not only the initial supply but also the subsequent maintenance of lifts and  elevators,"  she added.

Netherlands before 2004, and in the far smaller German escalator  market, but only between 1995 and

HD     (EU) EU/COMPETITION: Rumour is rife over condemnation of lift maker cartel

Brussels, 20/02/2007 (Agence Europe) - There have been various reports announcing the  Commission's likely condemnation, on Wednesday 21 February, of several lift and  escalator  manufacturers. After the Reuters report last Thursday (see EUROPE 9368), an article appeared in Le Monde of 19 February confirming that Competition Commissioner Neelie Kroes is soon to condemn the lift maker,  Otis (held by the American company, United Technologies), ThyseenKrupp (Germany),  Schindler (Switzerland) and Kone (Finland).

The Commissioner's spokespersons refused all comment. Nonetheless, a trade union representative of the companies charged admitted to Le Monde that “it would not come as a surprise” if the  Commission were to announce a  fine on Wednesday. Addressing EUROPE, a  Schindler spokesman confirmed this expectation.

TD
For several years, the sector has had to face allegations of unlawful  market sharing  between competitors. This agreement allowed them not only to impose high prices but also to undertake superfluous renovation work. Since 2003, complaints have been increasingly filed by cheap housing managers, and by tenants and joint owners of property. Furthermore, in 2005, several  Schindler employees were reportedly dismissed for unlawful agreements. It is not yet known to what extent these anecdotal incidents are connected to the current  Commission investigation.

Fines resulting from the investigation could, according to these sources, reach €1 billion. If this proves to be the case, it will be the heaviest  fine ever imposed on a cartel by the Commission, and will increase to
€1.75 billion the  fines imposed in 2007. We recall that €1.8 billion were imposed in fines in 2006, mainly after the June strengthening of anti-trust sanctions (see EUROPE 9221). (cd)

HD      EC Fines Lift Producers 992 Mln Euro For Price Fixing

Brussels/Vienna (APA/ADP) - The European  Commission (EC) has finedfine on four of the world's largest lift makers 992 mln euro ($1.303 bln) for operating cartels for the installation and maintenance of lifts and  escalators in Belgium, Germany, Luxembourg and the Netherlands, on February 21, 2007.

German steel and engineering group ThyssenKrupp AG was alone fined 479 mln euro ($629.62 mln), the largest fine the EC has ever imposed on a single company for cartel violations. The other  cartel participants are U.S. United Technologies Corp's  Otis, fined 225 mln euro ($295.75 mln), Swiss Schindler AG, 143.7 mln euro ($188.88 mln), and Finnish Kone Oyj, 142.1 mln euro ($186.78 mln).

TD
Between 1995 and 2004 the  cartel participants fixed bids for procurement contracts, fixed prices and allocated projects to each other, shared markets and exchanged commercially important and confidential information, the  Commission said.
The effects of the cartels are to be felt in the next years as well, EC added.

According to a spokesman of the European Commissioner for Competition Neelie Kroes, the EC limited its investigation on four countries, where there was evidence of collusion and it was up to the national anti-trust authorities to carry out further probes in other EU members. Kroes referred to one of the latest investigations of the Austrian anti-trust authority (BWB). On January 30, 2007, BWB filed a request for fines on leading lift and  escalator  manufacturers on suspicion of  cartel participation.

HD         EU Fines 4 Elevator Makers EUR992 Million  For Price Fixing
WC        543 words
PD         21 February 2007
ET          12:25 PM
SN         Dow Jones Capital Markets Report
SC         CM
LA          English
CY         (c) 2007 Dow Jones & Company, Inc.

LP
BRUSSELS (Dow Jones)--The European  Commission on Wednesday fined four elevator and
escalator  makers a total of EUR992 million for fixing prices, Europe's largest-ever antitrust penalty.

ThyssenKrupp AG (TKA.XE) was fined EUR479.7 million, United Technologies Corp.'s (UTX)  Otis unit was fined EUR224.9 million, Schindler Holding AG (SCHN.EB) was hit with a EUR143.7 million penalty and Kone was fined EUR142.1 million. [ 02-21-07 0642ET ]

TD
The sum of these  fines, which topped a EUR750 million penalty levied last month against makers of power-grid components, reflects the  commission's expanded powers to crack down on corporate cartels.

Under new antitrust guidelines established last year, the  commission can levy larger fines and apply an automatic 50% increase for repeat  cartel offenders. Since ThyssenKrupp had a previous  cartel punishment - a EUR3.2 million fine in 1998 for fixing stainless steel prices - its  elevator penalty included this hike, bringing its fine close to the record EUR497 million Microsoft Corp. (MSFT) paid in
2004 for breaking monopoly laws.

The commission launched its investigation into the  elevator business in January 2004. Its probe showed that the four companies fixed prices and rigged bids in Belgium, Germany, the Netherlands and Luxembourg between 1995 and 2004.

The commission said Mitsubishi Elevator Europe BV, a division of Mitsubishi Electric Corp. (6503.TO), participated in the Dutch cartel and it fined the company EUR1.8 million.

"It is outrageous that the construction and maintenance costs of buildings, including hospitals, have been artificially bloated by these cartels. The national management of these companies knew what they were doing was wrong, but they tried to conceal their action and went ahead anyway," the commission's top antitrust official Neelie Kroes said in a statement. [ 02-21-07 0712ET ]

In all four countries, high-ranking managers held regular meetings related to the  cartel. The commission found evidence that the companies knew their behavior was illegal. The managers were careful to avoid detection, meeting in rural areas or abroad and using prepaid mobile phone cards to avoid tracking.

The commission said none of the companies contested these facts or other evidence unearthed in the investigation.

None of the companies qualified for the full immunity usually given to whistle-blowers.  Kone's penalty was reduced by about EUR138 million because it was the first to disclose the cartels in Belgium and Luxembourg and provided key evidence in the German investigation.  Otis' penalty was reduced by over EUR208 million because it disclosed the Dutch  cartel and cooperated with investigations in the other countries.

Finland-based Kone and Germany's ThyssenKrupp said they will examine the commission's decision before deciding whether to appeal. Spokesmen for Switzerland-based Schindler and the German unit of U.S.-based  Otis declined to comment.



Page 52 of 169 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
A key question in this case will be whether the companies' customers decide to sue. Over the past few years, the commission has encouraged private damages claims to further deter  cartel offenders.
"Companies have the option of seeking damages" or renegotiating their contracts, said the
commission's antitrust spokesman Jonathan Todd.
-By Adam Cohen, Dow Jones Newswires; +322 741 1486; adam.cohen@dowjones.com [ 02-21-07
0820ET ]

(Updates an item, published from around 1125 GMT, with company responses and further details.)

BRUSSELS (Dow Jones)--The European Commission on Wednesday levied Europe's largest-ever antitrust penalty, fining five  elevator and escalator  makers a total of EUR992 million for fixing prices.

TD
ThyssenKrupp AG (TKA.XE) was fined EUR479.7 million, United Technologies Corp.'s (UTX)  Otis unit was fined EUR224.9 million, Schindler Holding AG (SCHN.EB) was hit with a EUR143.7 million penalty and Kone was fined EUR142.1 million.

The sum of these  fines, which topped a EUR790 million penalty levied in 2001 against makers of vitamin products, reflects the  Commission's expanded powers to crack down on corporate cartels.

Under new antitrust guidelines established last year, the  Commission can levy larger fines and apply an automatic 50% increase for repeat  cartel offenders. Since ThyssenKrupp had a previous  cartel punishment - a EUR3.2 million  fine in 1998 for fixing stainless steel prices - its  elevator penalty
included this added charge, bringing its  fine close to the record EUR497 million Microsoft Corp. (MSFT)
paid in 2004 for breaking monopoly laws.

"Three million euros is lunch money," said Julian Joshua, a lawyer at the Howrey LLP law firm in Brussels and a former deputy head of the  commission's cartel team. Steeper  fines, such as the ones in the elevator case, "can have important financial consequences for companies," he added.

ThyssenKrupp shares fell about 2% to EUR38.60 after the  fine was disclosed. The other three companies had smaller dips in their share prices.
The Commission launched its investigation into the  elevatorand escalator  businesses in January
2004. Its probe showed that the four companies fixed prices and rigged bids in Belgium, Germany, the
Netherlands and Luxembourg between 1995 and 2004.

The Commission said Mitsubishi Elevator Europe BV, a division of Mitsubishi Electric Corp. (6503.TO), participated in the Dutch cartel and fined the company EUR1.8 million.

"It is outrageous that the construction and maintenance costs of buildings, including hospitals, have been artificially bloated by these cartels. The national management of these companies knew what they were doing was wrong, but they tried to conceal their action and went ahead anyway," the Commission's top antitrust official Neelie Kroes said in a statement.

In all four countries, high-ranking managers held regular meetings related to the  cartel. The commission found evidence that the companies knew their behavior was illegal. The managers were careful to avoid detection, meeting in rural areas or abroad and using prepaid mobile phone cards to avoid tracking. The  Commission said none of the companies contested these facts or other evidence unearthed in the investigation.

None of the companies qualified for the full immunity usually given to whistle-blowers.  Kone's penalty was reduced by about EUR138 million because it was the first to disclose the cartels in Belgium and Luxembourg and provided key evidence in the German investigation.  Otis' penalty was reduced by over EUR208 million because it disclosed the Dutch  cartel and cooperated with investigations in the other countries.

Page 54 of 169 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
Otis said it will appeal the  Commission's decision at the European Court of First Instance in Luxembourg. Its parent company United Technologies said the  fine "will have no material impact" on its financial condition.

Schindler said it was "very surprised" at the size of the  fine since the companies were charged with local collusion, not running a pan-European  cartel.

Finland-based Kone and Germany's ThyssenKrupp said they will examine the  Commission's decision before deciding whether to appeal.

A key question in this case will be whether the companies' customers decide to sue. Over the past few years, the Commission has encouraged private damages claims to further deter  cartel offenders.
"Companies have the option of seeking damages" or renegotiating their contracts, said the
Commission's antitrust spokesman Jonathan Todd.

Feb 21, 2007 (DJCS via Comtex) --

In all four countries, high-ranking managers held regular meetings related to the  cartel. The commission found evidence that the companies knew their behavior was illegal. The managers were careful to avoid detection, meeting in rural areas or abroad and using prepaid mobile phone cards to avoid tracking.

TD
The commission said none of the companies contested these facts or other evidence unearthed in the investigation.

Kone's penalty was reduced by about EUR138 million because it was the first to disclose the cartels in Belgium and Luxembourg and provided key evidence in the German investigation.  Otis' penalty was reduced by over EUR208 million because it disclosed the Dutch  cartel and cooperated with investigations in the other countries.

Finland-based Kone and Germany's ThyssenKrupp said they will examine the commission's decision before deciding whether to appeal. Spokesmen for Switzerland-based Schindler and the German unit of U.S.-based  Otis declined to comment.

A key question in this case will be whether the companies' customers decide to sue. Over the past few years, the commission has encouraged private damages claims to further deter  cartel offenders.
"Companies have the option of seeking damages" or renegotiating their contracts, said the
commission's antitrust spokesman Jonathan Todd.

HD         DJ UPDATE: EU Fines Elevator Makers EUR992M For Price-Fixing
WC        675 words
PD         21 February 2007
ET         05:33 PM
SN         Dow Jones Commodities Service
SC         OSTDJ
LA          English
CY         Copyright 2007, Comtex News Network. All Rights Reserved.

LP
BRUSSELS, Feb 21, 2007 (DJCS via Comtex) --
(Updates an item, published from around 1125 GMT, with company responses and further details.)

TD
The European Commission on Wednesday levied Europe's largest-ever antitrust penalty, fining five
elevator and escalator  makers a total of EUR992 million for fixing prices.

ThyssenKrupp AG (TKA.XE) was fined EUR479.7 million, United Technologies Corp.'s (UTX)  Otis unit was fined EUR224.9 million, Schindler Holding AG (SCHN.EB) was hit with a EUR143.7 million penalty and Kone was fined EUR142.1 million.

The sum of these  fines, which topped a EUR790 million penalty levied in 2001 against makers of vitamin products, reflects the  Commission's expanded powers to crack down on corporate cartels.

Under new antitrust guidelines established last year, the  Commission can levy larger fines and apply an automatic 50% increase for repeat  cartel offenders. Since ThyssenKrupp had a previous  cartel punishment - a EUR3.2 million  fine in 1998 for fixing stainless steel prices - its  elevator penalty
included this added charge, bringing its  fine close to the record EUR497 million Microsoft Corp. (MSFT)
paid in 2004 for breaking monopoly laws.

"Three million euros is lunch money," said Julian Joshua, a lawyer at the Howrey LLP law firm in Brussels and a former deputy head of the  commission's cartel team. Steeper  fines, such as the ones in the elevator case, "can have important financial consequences for companies," he added.

ThyssenKrupp shares fell about 2% to EUR38.60 after the  fine was disclosed. The other three companies had smaller dips in their share prices.
The Commission launched its investigation into the  elevatorand escalator  businesses in January
2004. Its probe showed that the four companies fixed prices and rigged bids in Belgium, Germany, the
Netherlands and Luxembourg between 1995 and 2004.

The Commission said Mitsubishi Elevator Europe BV, a division of Mitsubishi Electric Corp. (6503.TO), participated in the Dutch cartel and fined the company EUR1.8 million.

"It is outrageous that the construction and maintenance costs of buildings, including hospitals, have been artificially bloated by these cartels. The national management of these companies knew what they were doing was wrong, but they tried to conceal their action and went ahead anyway," the Commission's top antitrust official Neelie Kroes said in a statement.

In all four countries, high-ranking managers held regular meetings related to the  cartel. The commission found evidence that the companies knew their behavior was illegal. The managers were careful to avoid detection, meeting in rural areas or abroad and using prepaid mobile phone cards to avoid tracking. The  Commission said none of the companies contested these facts or other evidence unearthed in the investigation.

None of the companies qualified for the full immunity usually given to whistle-blowers.  Kone's penalty was reduced by about EUR138 million because it was the first to disclose the cartels in Belgium and Luxembourg and provided key evidence in the German investigation.  Otis' penalty was reduced by over

Page 59 of 169 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
EUR208 million because it disclosed the Dutch  cartel and cooperated with investigations in the other countries.

Otis said it will appeal the  Commission's decision at the European Court of First Instance in Luxembourg. Its parent company United Technologies said the  fine "will have no material impact" on its financial condition.

Schindler said it was "very surprised" at the size of the  fine since the companies were charged with local collusion, not running a pan-European  cartel.

Finland-based Kone and Germany's ThyssenKrupp said they will examine the  Commission's decision before deciding whether to appeal.

A key question in this case will be whether the companies' customers decide to sue. Over the past few years, the Commission has encouraged private damages claims to further deter  cartel offenders.
"Companies have the option of seeking damages" or renegotiating their contracts, said the
Commission's antitrust spokesman Jonathan Todd.
-By Adam Cohen, Dow Jones Newswires; +322 741 1486; adam.cohen@dowjones.com
(END) Dow Jones Newswires
02-21-07 1131ET
