HD           CHECK URGED AFTER LIFT MANUFACTURERS' CARTEL SCAM EXPOSED WC          660 words
PD           21 February 2007
ET            06:58 PM
SN           Press Association National Newswire
SC           PRESSA
LA            English
CY           (c)2007, The Press Association, All Rights Reserved

LP
By Geoff Meade, PA Europe editor, Brussels

The Government was urged today to check whether a massive scam run by a  cartel of lift manufacturers on the continent has also hit British taxpayers, public authorities and property developers.

TD
The call came after the European Commission imposed fines of more than £665 million on four
major makers of lifts and escalators for illegally rigging prices in Belgium, Germany, Luxembourg and
the Netherlands.
For at least a decade public and private companies have been 'ripped off, big time'' said  Commission
spokesman Jonathan Todd.

The cartel colluded to fix the cost of projects installing and maintaining lifts and  escalators for everything from hospitals and railway stations to shopping centres and commercial buildings.
Even the European  Commission's own Brussels headquarters and the European Court of Justice in
Luxembourg were victims of the scams operated by  Otis, Schindler, ThyssenKrupp and  KONE.

The web of secretly-fixed procurement contracts and rigged prices could extend across the EU, warned Mr Todd.

'Taxpayers, public authorities and property developers have been ripped off big time. Prices were way above what they should have been. Today's decision (to impose  fines) is based on information we have been able to prove,'' he said.

'This does not mean that the  cartel activity did not take place in other member states. It simply means we did not have sufficient proof on which to base a decision.''
He added: 'It is up to the competition authorities in each member state to verify whether this  cartel
activity may also have taken place on their territory.''

EU Competition Commissioner Neelie Kroes said: 'It is outrageous that the construction and maintenance costs of buildings, including hospitals, have been artificially bloated by these cartels.

'The damage caused will last for many years because these cartels covered not only the initial supply but also the subsequent maintenance of lifts and  escalators for these companies: the memory of this fine should last just as long.''

Liberal Democrat MEP Sharon Bowles said: 'The behaviour of these companies is only made worse by the fact that some of their contracts were made with hospitals where every single penny counts.
'These companies have a responsibility to offer real genuine prices for their goods and services.''

Mr Todd explained how the dominant firms in the cartels rigged the market so that they all got a share. Three of the four in the  cartel club would bid wildly above the fourth, more or less ensuring that the already-inflated lowest bid won the tender.

Page 61 of 169 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
On another contract, another member of the  cartel would be selected to submit the lowest bid.

Senior management staff of the companies - managing directors, sales and services directors, and heads of customer services departments, would meet regularly and in secret.

'There is evidence that the companies were aware that their behaviour was illegal and they took care to avoid detection,'' said a  Commission statement.

'They usually met in bars and restaurants, they travelled to the countryside or even abroad and they used pre-paid mobile phone cards to avoid tracking.''

The effects of the scams could last as long as 50 years because of the maintenance contracts tied to the installation deals - although affected firms could now challenge the contracts through national courts.

The fact that the  Commission itself and the European Court were victims of the scams emerged as investigations went on.
'My understanding is that the contract for the provision of  elevators  and escalators in this building
(EU Commission HQ) and in the Court of Justice were indeed covered by the cartel,'' said Mr Todd.
That could be unfortunate for the companies facing the largest collective  fine ever imposed by
Brussels - because their only appeal is to the European Court.

If the  fines do stand, EU taxpayers will benefit, because the money is put into the EU budget, reducing the member states' annual contributions.

HD          EU Fines Finnish  Kone 142 Mln Euro in Cartel Case
WC         171 words
PD          21 February 2007
SN          Finnish News Digest
SC          FINNND
LA          English
CY          (c) 2007 AII Data Processing Ltd. All Rights Reserved.

LP
The EU Commission imposed a 142 mln euro ($186.5 mln)  fine on Finnish company Kone Oyj for anticompetitive practices in the  elevator and escalator  market in Germany and the Netherlands,  Kone said on February 21, 2007.

The ruling is the result of a three-year investigation into price-fixing in the elevator and escalator market in Europe, started in 2004. The  Commission had found Kone's subsidiaries in Belgium, Luxembourg, Germany and the Netherlands were involved in local anticompetitive practices prior to
2004.

TD
However, the company did not receive a  fine for Belgium and Luxembourg as it was the first to cooperate with the  Commission regarding these countries.

Kone said it would examine the  Commission's decision and would decide on potential further action.

Kone manufactures  elevators  and escalators, and provides maintenance of automatic building doors. The company generated net sales of 3.6 bln euro ($4.742 bln) for 2006.

www.kone.com

ULRICH MIDDELMANN, CFO, THYSSENKRUPP AG: Hello, ladies and gentlemen. This is Ulrich Middelmann speaking. I welcome you to this afternoon's conference call on the  fines of the E.U. Commission has imposed on elevator companies. Welcome also to Internet listeners who are following the online audio transmission.

I am sure that you are already aware of the reason for this call. Today the E.U.  Commission announced that it has imposed  fines on several elevator companies, including our Group segment ThyssenKrupp Elevator AG. The various elevator companies - Kone, Otis, Schindler and Mitsubishi - are accused of having reached price-fixing agreements for elevators  and escalators. These agreements were made at national level in the Benelux countries and Germany.

ThyssenKrupp has incurred a  fine in the amount of roughly EUR480m. As ThyssenKrupp has not yet received the complete notice of the  fine, we cannot yet fully assess whether it is justified. The ThyssenKrupp legal team will review the  fine notice and then decide whether to appeal. Although we did not have all the relevant information at present, our intention was to inform you as quickly as possible.
In the following I will shed some more light on what has happened, what the E.U. has done up to now, how
ThyssenKrupp has cooperated and how this  fine might affect our current year's financials.

I would like to start with the latter. The  fine definitely represents a significant amount. Nevertheless, since the financial structure of the Group is in excellent and rock-solid shape, the  fine will not cause financial problems for us. EBT guidance will be unaffected since it is, as you know, before major non-recurring items. The  fine will affect our bottom line in the second quarter but as a major non-recurring item. It will be
presented in the line item Other Operating Expenses in our income statement and will be allocated in full to our elevator segment.

The investment program that you know from the investor relations presentation will be unaffected. The investment grade rating is not in question, of course. The  fine of roughly EUR480m will fall due for payment three months after ThyssenKrupp has received the final decision of the E.U.  Commission. If ThyssenKrupp should decide to appeal against the  fine, there is also the option to provide a bank guarantee in order to avoid a cash outflow on due date.
Page 64 of 169 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
Ladies and gentlemen, let's turn to the past for a moment again. On October 11, 2005 the European Commission announced the initiation of administrative  fine proceedings against the aforementioned companies in the elevator and escalator  industry. This also affected some European subsidiaries of ThyssenKrupp Elevator AG.
Prior to the initiation of the administrative  fine proceedings, the European  Commission had conducted
pre-investigations in Germany, Belgium, The Netherlands and Luxemburg at the beginning of 2004. As part
of these pre-investigations, several dawn raids were carried out at the premises of  Kone, Schindler, Otis,
ThyssenKrupp and Mitsubishi in the European Union and at the corresponding associations. The subject of
the administrative  fine proceedings is that the respective companies are accused of having violated
European antitrust law in connection with the manufacturing and servicing of  elevators  and escalators in
certain member states of the European Union.

ThyssenKrupp has been cooperating with the antitrust authorities and started internal investigations across Europe at a very early stage to support the investigation process of the  cartel authorities. In the course of our own investigations, we discovered that there had been breaches of conduct by individual employees in local markets of the  elevator segment. However, there was no Europe-wide agreement and no systematic wrongdoing.

As a result, ThyssenKrupp took personal action and consequently individual executives in the markets concerned had to leave the Company and are no longer with ThyssenKrupp. It goes without saying that part of our cooperation with the E.U.  Commission involved informing them of the results of the internal examination.

For the future, we have taken every step to avoid breaches of conduct at both ThyssenKrupp  Elevator AG and in the ThyssenKrupp Group. Therefore we are convinced that there will be no recurrence of these unfortunate events.

At the balance sheet date, end of September 2006, we did not make any financial provisions as the E.U. Commission's investigations had not yet taken such concrete form as to allow any reasonable assessment of possible financial consequences.

Ladies and gentlemen, a ThyssenKrupp legal team will review the  fine notice and then decide whether to appeal. It goes without saying that we will keep the capital markets informed about any new development in this matter.

BRUSSELS, Feb. 21 (Xinhua) -- The European  Commission on Wednesday handed down a record antitrust  fine of nearly a billion euros over several lift companies for running illegal cartels.
The lift makers  Otis, Kone, Schindler and ThyssenKrupp groups were given a combined penalty worth
992 million euros, the biggest ever fine imposed by the European Union's antitrust watchdog for  cartel
violations.

TD
Those major lift companies was accused of operating cartels for the installation and maintenance of lifts and escalators in Belgium, Germany, Luxembourg and the Netherlands between 1995 and 2004.

The investigation revealed that they rigged bids for procurement contracts, fixed prices and allocated projects to each other, shared markets and exchanged commercially important and confidential information.

"The damage caused by this  cartel will last for many years because it covered not only the initial supply but also the subsequent maintenance of lifts and  elevators  -- for these companies the memory of this fine should last just as long," the EU Competition Commissioner Neelie Kroes said.

HD         Update: EU imposes  biggest  fine on lift companies for cartel
WC        389 words
PD         21 February 2007
SN         Xinhua News Agency
SC         XNEWS
LA         English
CY         (c) Copyright 2007 Xinhua News Agency

LP
BRUSSELS, Feb. 21 (Xinhua) -- The European  Commission on Wednesday handed down a record antitrust  fine of almost a billion euros over several lift companies for running illegal cartels.

The lift and  elevator makers Otis, Kone, Schindler and ThyssenKrupp groups were given a combined penalty worth 992 million euros, the biggest ever fine imposed by the European Union 's antitrust watchdog for  cartel violations.

TD
Those major lift companies was accused of operating cartels for the installation and maintenance of lifts and escalators in Belgium,Germany, Luxembourg and the Netherlands between 1995 and 2004.

The investigation revealed that they rigged bids for procurement contracts, fixed prices and allocated projects to each other, shared markets and exchanged commercially important and confidential information.

Even the European Commission's headquarters in Brussels and the EU courts building in Luxembourg were affected by their cartel arrangement.

The effects of this  cartel may continue for 20 to 50 years as maintenance is often done by the companies that installed the equipment in the first place, and by cartelising the installation, the companies distorted the markets for years to come, the  commission said.
Among those companies, ThyssenKrupp alone had to pay almost half of the total  fine, the heaviest
ever imposed by the commission on a single company in a cartel case. The German group' s  fine was
increased by 50 percent to 480 million euros because it was a repeated offender.

Otis, a unit of U.S. conglomerate United Technologies Corp., received a  fine of 225 million euros, followed by Switzerland- based  Schindler with a penalty of 144 million euros and  Kone of Finland with
142 million euros.
Japanese company Mitsubishi was also  fined 1.8 million euros for its limited role in the Dutch  cartel. "The damage caused by this  cartel will last for many years because it covered not only the initial supply
but also the subsequent maintenance of lifts and  elevators  - for these companies the memory of this
fine should last just as long," the EU Competition Commissioner Neelie Kroes said.

The European  Commission imposed a total  fine of 855 million euros on eight vitamin makers in 2001, the biggest penalty in an EU  cartel case before Wednesday's decision. The  fine was later reduced to
790 million euros by an EU court.

SE            Business: World
HD            Lift manufacturers fined €992 for price fixing
WC           373 words
PD            21 February 2007
ET             03:42 PM
SN            The Irish Examiner
SC            IRISEX
LA             English
CY            © Irish Examiner, 2007. Thomas Crosbie Media, TCH

LP
European Union regulators slapped their largest  price-fixing fine ever – €992m – on five lift makers today.
The was imposed for operating cartels for the installation and maintenance of lifts and  escalators in
Germany, Belgium, Luxembourg and the Netherlands.

TD
EU spokesman Jonathan Todd said in Brussels that the  fines were the "largest ever" for  price fixing
in the European Union.

"The result of this  cartel is that taxpayers, public authorities and property developers have been ripped off big time," Todd said.

"The companies ensured that by rigging the bids and sharing the markets that the prices paid for the installation and the maintenance were way above what they would have been if there had been a competitive market."

German conglomerate ThyssenKrupp, Finland’s  Kone and Switzerland’s  Schindler Holding were fined under the decision, as was United Technologies unit  Otis Elevator of the United States and subsidiaries of Japan’s Mitsubishi  Elevator Europe.
ThyssenKrupp was given the largest  fine, more than €479m, because the company was labelled a
"repeat offender" by EU regulators, Mr Todd said.
The company said in a statement that it would review the  fine and then decide whether to appeal.

Otis was fined €225m, Schindler €144m, Kone €142m, and Mitsubishi’s Dutch subsidiary was  fined
€1.8m.

The European  Commission said the cartel had worked to rig bids for procurement contracts, fix prices and share markets between at least 1995 and 2004. The  Commission said the companies
"did not contest the facts" found by EU regulators, adding none of the accused requested a hearing to answer the allegations.
But Schindler said it was "very surprised at the size of the  fine," because it claimed the EU had
found "no evidence of pan-European collusion among companies in the European  elevator industry".
It said in a statement that it had not yet decided whether it would appeal.

Kone also said it will "examine the  commission’s decision and decide on potential action."

Today’s fine tops a €750m fine imposed last month against 10 companies for running a  cartel to fix prices for heavy equipment used by power utilities, reflecting stepped-up action by EU Competition Commissioner Neelie Kroes against  price fixing.

BRUSSELS, Feb. 21 -- The European  Commission on Wednesday imposed a record total of 992 million euro in fines on 14 elevator makers, including Mitsubishi  Elevator Europe, for forming cartels.

''It is outrageous that the construction and maintenance costs of buildings, including hospitals, have been artificially bloated by these cartels,'' Competition Commissioner Neelie Kroes said in announcing the fine against price fixing  in the European Union.

TD
''The national management of these companies knew what they were doing was wrong, but they tried to conceal their actions and went ahead anyway.''

Among the companies, Germany's ThyssenKrupp was  fined about 480 million euro, while  Otis          Elevator and Schindler Elevator, both from the United States, were  fined 225 million euro and 143 million euro respectively.
Mitsubishi Elevator from Japan was fined 1.84 million euro.

The companies rigged bids for procurement contracts, fixed prices and allocated projects to each other for the installation and management of  elevators  and escalators between 1995 and 2004, the commission said.

HD          UPDATE 4-EU fines elevator cartel a record 992 mln euros
BY          By David Lawsky
WC         500 words
PD          21 February 2007
ET          07:02 AM
SN          Reuters News
SC          LBA
LA           English
CY          (c) 2007 Reuters Limited

LP
(Adds Otis reaction)

BRUSSELS, Feb 21 (Reuters) - The European  Commission fined lift manufacturers a record 992 million euros ($1.3 billion) on Wednesday for fixing prices and carving up markets in the biggest  cartel penalty in European Union history.

TD
Germany's ThyssenKrupp incurred the highest ever  fine for a single company -- 480 million euros ($630.6 million) -- raised by 50 percent because it was a repeat offender. The company said it would decide whether to appeal.

The companies had fixed prices, rigged bids and allocated projects to each other in Belgium, Germany, Luxembourg and the Netherlands between 1995 and 2004, the EU's competition regulator said.

"The result of this cartel is that taxpayers, public authorities and property developers have been ripped off big time," Commission competition spokesman Jonathan Todd said.

"Its effects will be felt for the next 20 to 50 years, because a lot of these companies make their money from the maintenance which is done following the installation of the  escalators and elevators,"  he told a news conference.

Todd invited companies that had suffered at the hands of the  cartel to pursue damages in national courts and seek to annul and renegotiate long-term service contracts.

The huge fines reflected the scale of the market, the size of the companies and the long-term effects, he said.
Among the many buildings affected were the headquarters of the European  Commission, the
Berlaymont, from which Todd was speaking, and the European Union courts building in Luxembourg.
IDENTIFIED AND DISMISSED
The Commission fined Otis, owned by United Technologies of the United States, 225 million euros. "The nine local  Otis employees who violated our code of ethics and regulatory compliance
programmes were quickly identified and dismissed," Chairman and Chief Executive George David said
in a statement.

Kone of Finland was fined 142 million euros and said: "We have only received a short release and know the amount of the fine and are expecting more information."

Schindler of Switzerland was fined 144 million euros.  Schindler said it was "very surprised at the size of the fine since the European Commission found no evidence of pan-European collusion."  Schindler said the Commission had looked for pan-European evidence but dropped the accusation.

Mitsubishi Electric Corp of Japan was  fined 1.8 million euros for participating in the Dutch portion of the cartel.

Page 76 of 169 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
Competition Commissioner Neelie Kroes was unapologetic for the size of the  fines.

"It is outrageous that the construction and maintenance costs of buildings, including hospitals, have been artificially bloated by these cartels," she said in a statement.

"The national management of these companies knew what they were doing was wrong, but they tried to conceal their action and went ahead anyway. The damage caused by this  cartel will last for many years," she said.

HELSINKI, Feb 21 (Reuters) - Finnish lift and  escalator  maker Kone said it would make a first-quarter provision for a European  Commission fine of 142 million euro ($187 million) for  price-fixing, but had yet to decide on any appeal.

TD

Kone spokeswoman Minna Mars said Kone would book the fine as an one-off cost in its first-quarter earnings, regardless of whether appeals or simply decides to pay the  fine.

Kone said it was fined for anticompetitive practices in Germany and the Netherlands, but not penalised in relation to Belgium and Luxembourg, where it had been the first company to cooperate with the  Commission in its investigations.

Kone's Cawen said the lift maker had made efforts to increase its employees' knowledge on competition law and carried out an internal investigation at the time in 2004, when the case became public.
The Commission fined Kone and four other lift manufacturers a total of 992 million euros for
price-fixing.
The others include ThyssenKrupp of Germany,  Otis, owned by United Technologies of the United
States,  Schindler of Switzerland and Mitsubishi Electric of Japan.

Kone shares were 0.4 percent lower at 45.73 euros by 1501 GMT, outperforming the Helsinki bourse general index <.OMXHPI>, down 0.6 percent.

HD         UPDATE 1-ThyssenKrupp says fine won't hurt Elevator margins
BY         By Christiaan Hetzner
WC        541 words
PD         21 February 2007
ET         05:26 PM
SN         Reuters News
SC         LBA
LA          English
CY         (c) 2007 Reuters Limited

LP
(Adds comments from conference call with management)

FRANKFURT, Feb 21 (Reuters) - German industrial conglomerate ThyssenKrupp does not expect profitability at its high-margin Elevator unit to decline as competition returns to normal following an EU crackdown on a price-fixing cartel.

TD
Earlier, the EU  Commission levied a record 480 million euro ($630.6 million) fine on the company for its role in a scheme during the mid-nineties to 2004 that also included Schindler , Kone , Mitsubishi Electric Corp and United Technologies subsidiary  Otis.

"We do not see that the contracts which we had negotiated after all those things became obvious were negatively affected. so we do not expect in the future a deterioration of our margin or profitability in the elevator segment," Claus Ehrenbeck, head of investor relations, told a conference call with analysts.

ThyssenKrupp Chief Financial Officer Ulrich Middelmann said the breach was a one-off, adding it would not happen again.

"In the course of our own (internal) investigations we discovered that there had been breaches of conduct by individual employees in local markets of the  Elevator segment. However there was no Europe-wide agreement and no systematic wrongdoing," Middelmann told the analysts.

The Commission's competition spokesman, Jonathan Todd, had earlier invited companies that had suffered at the hands the  cartel to pursue damages in national courts and seek to annul and renegotiate long-term service contracts.

Maintenance contracts, often concluded after installation, usually last several years and are a lucrative business providing stable cash flow.
Middelmann said it was difficult to say how many maintenance contracts would be affected by the EU
finding and that the group would have to consider this over the next couple of weeks.
REPEAT OFFENDER

"Our average length of contracts for service activities is between five to 10 years, so we need to have a detailed investigation which contracts might be affected. You know that the time of the investigation of the EU Commission is quite old in some parts so we need further clarification," he said.

When asked to what possible extent they expected damage claims or recourse from customers, ThyssenKrupp general counsel Thomas Kremer said: "We just have to assess the situation how it is ... up to now we have no indication that any indemnity claim will be raised."

Middelmann said he expected some possibility to reduce the size of the  fine, in which ThyssenKrupp suffered a 50 percent additional penalty for being a repeat offender.

Since its  Elevator segment only generated 4.3 billion euros in sales last year, the 480 million euros exceeded the general maximum limit for  fines of 10 percent of revenue.

Page 82 of 169 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
The group's forecast of significantly more than 2.5 billion euros in pretax profit in the current fiscal year
2006/07 to the end of September will not be affected, it said, since the target is before major
non-recurring items. The fine will affect the company's bottom line in the second quarter and would be
fully allocated to the Elevators  segment.
ThyssenKrupp said it is considering whether to appeal against the  cartel finding.

HD     EU slaps €992 million (US$1.3 billion) fine on elevator makers for price fixing

BY      By CONSTANT BRAND CR     Associated Press Writer WC    786 words
PD     21 February 2007
ET      04:22 PM
SN     Associated Press Newswires
SC     APRS
LA      English
CY     (c) 2007. The Associated Press. All Rights Reserved.

LP
BRUSSELS, Belgium (AP) - European Union regulators slapped their largest  price-fixing fine ever --
€992 million (US$1.3 billion) -- on five elevator makers Wednesday for operating cartels for the
installation and maintenance of lifts and escalators in Germany, Belgium, Luxembourg and the
Netherlands.
EU spokesman Jonathan Todd said the  fines were the "largest ever" for  price fixing  in the European
Union.

TD
"The result of this  cartel is that taxpayers, public authorities and property developers have been ripped off big time," Todd told reporters. "The companies ensured that by rigging the bids and sharing the markets that the prices paid for the installation and the maintenance were way above what they would have been if there had been a competitive market."

German conglomerate ThyssenKrupp AG, Finland's  Kone Corp. and Switzerland's Schindler Holding AG were fined under the decision, as was United Technologies Corp.'s unit Otis Elevator Co. of the United States and subsidiaries of Japan's Mitsubishi  Elevator Europe BV.

ThyssenKrupp was given the largest  fine, over €479 million (US$630 million), because the company was labeled a "repeat offender" by EU regulators, Todd said.
The company said in a statement that it will review the  fine and then decide whether to appeal.

Otis was fined €225 million (US$295.8 million),  Schindler €144 million (US$189.3 million),  Kone €142 million (US$187 million), and Mitsubishi's Dutch subsidiary was  fined €1.8 million (US$2.37 million).

The European  Commission said the cartel had worked to rig bids for procurement contracts, fix prices and share markets between at least 1995 and 2004. The  Commission said the companies "did not contest the facts" found by EU regulators, adding none of the accused requested a hearing to answer the allegations.

But Schindler said it was "very surprised at the size of the  fine," because it claimed the EU had found "no evidence of pan-European collusion among companies in the European  elevator industry." It said in a statement that it had not yet decided whether it would appeal.

Otis, however, said it would appeal. "Otis and UTC first disclosed this investigation in early 2004, have cooperated fully with the European Commission, and received full or partial reductions in penalties in all jurisdictions for this cooperation," the company said in a statement.

Kone, the world's fourth largest elevator and escalator  maker, also said it had cooperated with Commission officials in the cartel investigation as soon as they heard about it in January 2004. It said it will "examine the commission's decision and decide on potential action."




Page 84 of 169 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
Wednesday's fine tops a €750 million (US$978 million)  fine imposed last month against 10 companies for running a cartel to fix prices for heavy equipment used by power utilities, reflecting stepped-up action by EU Competition Commissioner Neelie Kroes against  price fixing.

She has vowed a crackdown under new antitrust guidelines, which allow EU regulators to come down harder on repeat offenders like ThyssenKrupp, which faces an automatic 50 percent increase for repeated price-fixing activities.

The German company had already received a previous  cartel fine of €3.2 million (US$4.2 million) in 1998 for fixing stainless steel prices. Wednesday's punishment brings ThyssenKrupp close to the €479 million (US$613 million)  fine paid by U.S. software giant Microsoft Corp. for breaking European monopoly laws.

EU regulators found evidence from "numerous documents and corporate statements" to prove the companies allocated tenders and contracts for the sale, installation and maintenance of  elevators  "with the aim of freezing market shares and fixing prices," the  Commission said.

"Projects that were rigged included lifts and  escalators for hospitals, railway stations, shopping centers and commercial buildings," it said, adding such acts were decided upon between managing and service directors during regular meetings between  cartel members.

"There is evidence that the companies were aware that their behavior was illegal and they took care to avoid detection," the  Commission said, adding that the meetings usually took place in bars and restaurants or in secret locations outside cities to avoid suspicion.
EU officials said they even used prepaid mobile phone cards to avoid tracking.

"The national management of these companies know what they were doing was wrong, but they tried to conceal their action and went ahead anyway," Kroes said in a statement. "The damage caused by this cartel will last for many years because it covered not only the initial supply but also the subsequent maintenance of lifts and  elevators."

The EU conducted surprise raids against the companies in January 2004 after EU regulators were tipped off.

HD     EU slaps $1.3 billion cartel fine on United Technologies' Otis unit, 4 other elevator makers

BY      By CONSTANT BRAND CR     Associated Press Writer WC    879 words
PD     21 February 2007
ET      04:07 PM
SN     Associated Press Newswires
SC     APRS
LA      English
CY     (c) 2007. The Associated Press. All Rights Reserved.

LP
BRUSSELS, Belgium (AP) - European Union regulators on Wednesday  fined United Technologies'  Otis unit and four other elevator makers $1.3 billion for operating cartels for the installation and maintenance of elevators  and escalators in Germany, Belgium, Luxembourg and the Netherlands.

EU spokesman Jonathan Todd said the  fines represented the "largest ever" for  price fixing  in the European Union, and EU Competition Commissioner Neelie Kroes said the costs of buildings and hospitals were "artificially bloated by these cartels."

TD
"The result of this  cartel is that taxpayers, public authorities and property developers have been ripped off big time," Todd told reporters. "The companies ensured that by rigging the bids and sharing the markets that the prices paid for the installation and the maintenance were way above what they would have been if there had been a competitive market."

Fined were Hartford, Conn.-based United Technologies Corp., the corporate parent of Otis Elevator; German conglomerate ThyssenKrupp AG; Finland's  Kone Corp.; Switzerland's Schindler Holding AG and subsidiaries of Japan's Mitsubishi  Elevator Europe BV.

George David, chairman and chief executive of United Technologies, said in a statement that company officials are "disappointed by the conduct revealed through the  Commission's and UTC's own investigations."

Otis was fined 225 million euros ($295.8 million).

"UTC will not tolerate unethical behaviors of any kind," he said. "The nine local  Otis employees who violated our code of ethics and regulatory compliance programs were quickly identified and dismissed and we have reinvigorated our ethics and compliance programs worldwide."

Otis and United Technologies disclosed the investigation in 2004 and have cooperated with the European Commission, David said.  Otis and UTC have received full or partial reductions in penalties for its cooperation and the outcome will have "no material impact" on UTC's financial condition or  Otis' competitive position.

United Technologies is waiting for the full text of the decision and will review it before reporting on its impact on its 2007 earnings guidance. In addition, David said  Otis will appeal the decision to a European court.

ThyssenKrupp was given the largest  fine, just over 479 million euros ($630 million), because the company was labeled a "repeat offender" by EU regulators, Todd said.
Frankfurt, Germany-based ThyssenKrupp said it will review the  fine and then decide whether to appeal.

Schindler 144 million euros ($189.3 million),  Kone 142 million euros ($187 million) and Mitsubishi's
Dutch subsidiary was  fined 1.8 million euros ($2.37 million).


Page 88 of 169 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
The European  Commission said the cartel had worked to rig bids for procurement contracts, fix prices and share markets "between at least 1995 and 2004." It said the companies "did not contest the facts" found by EU regulators, adding none of the accused requested a hearing to answer the allegations.

But Schindler said the company was "very surprised at the size of the  fine," because it claimed the EU had found "no evidence of pan-European collusion among companies in the European  elevator industry." It said in a statement that it had not yet decided whether it would appeal.

Kone also said it will "examine the  commission's decision and decide on potential action."

Wednesday's fine tops a 750 million euros ($978 million)  fine imposed last month against 10 companies for running a cartel to fix prices for heavy equipment used by power utilities, reflecting stepped-up action by Kroes against price fixing.

She has vowed a crackdown under new antitrust guidelines agreed to last year that allow EU regulators to come down harder on repeat offenders like ThyssenKrupp, which faces an automatic 50 percent increase for repeated  price-fixing activities.

The German company had already received a previous  cartel fine, worth 3.2 million euros ($4.2 million) in 1998 for fixing stainless steel prices. Wednesday's punishment brings ThyssenKrupp close to the 479 million euros fine paid by U.S. software giant Microsoft Corp. for breaking European monopoly laws.

EU regulators found evidence from "numerous documents and corporate statements" to prove the companies allocated tenders and contracts for the sale, installation and maintenance of  elevators  "with the aim of freezing market shares and fixing prices," the  commission said.

"Projects that were rigged included lifts and  escalators for hospitals, railway stations, shopping centers and commercial buildings," it said, adding such acts were decided upon between managing and service directors during regular meetings between  cartel members.

"There is evidence that the companies were aware that their behavior was illegal and they took care to avoid detection," the  commission said, adding that the meetings usually took place in bars and restaurants or in secret locations outside cities to avoid suspicion.
EU officials said they even used prepaid mobile phone cards to avoid tracking.

"The national management of these companies know what they were doing was wrong, but they tried to conceal their action and went ahead anyway," Kroes said in a statement. "The damage caused by this cartel will last for many years because it covered not only the initial supply but also the subsequent maintenance of lifts and  elevators."

The EU conducted surprise raids against the companies in January 2004 after EU regulators were tipped off.

HD        EU Slaps Record Near Billion-euro Fine on Lift Cartel
WC       596 words
PD        21 February 2007
ET         03:57 PM
SN        Deutsche Welle
SC        DEUEN
LA         English
CY        (c) 2007. Deutsche Welle.

LP
EU competition regulators imposed their biggest  fine ever on Wednesday, hitting lift makers Otis,
Schindler, ThyssenKrupp and  Kone with a penalty of nearly a billion euros for running an illegal cartel.

The European Commission accused the companies of operating a  cartel for the installation and maintenance of lifts and escalators in Belgium, Germany, Luxembourg and The Netherlands between at least 1995 and 2004.

TD
The European Union's antitrust watchdog said it was fining them a combined 992 million euros ($1.3 billion) for rigging bids for procurement contracts, fixing prices, sharing out projects,
carving up markets and exchanging sensitive information.

The total fine also included a 1.8-million-euro penalty against Japanese company Mitsubishi for a role in the cartel limited to The Netherlands.

"It is outrageous that the construction and maintenance costs of buildings, including hospitals, have been artificially bloated by
these cartels," Competition Commissioner Neelie Kroes said.

"The national management of these companies knew what they were doing was wrong, but they tried to conceal their action and went ahead anyway," she said. "The damage caused by this  cartel will last for many years because it covered not only the initial supply but also the subsequent maintenance of lifts and elevators  -- for these companies the memory of this  fine should last just as long," she added.
German firm gets stiffest  fine

German steelmaker ThyssenKrupp was handed the heaviest  fine ever imposed by EU regulators on a single company in a cartel case because it was a repeated offender. The German group was  fined 480 million euros.

Otis, a unit of US conglomerate United Technologies Corporation, was given a  fine of 225 million euros for its role, followed by  Schindler with a penalty of 144 million euros and  Kone with 142 million euros.

EU antitrust investigators conducted so-called "dawn raids" on lift and  escalator  makers across Europe in January 2004 after getting a tip off about the cartel. After the raids, the companies handed over more evidence in the hope of winning leniency or reduced fines.

The European  Commission said that high-ranking national-level management ran the  cartel by holding secret meetings in bars and restaurants. They also visited the countryside and traveled abroad and
used pre-paid mobile phone cards to avoid regulators' scrutiny.
EU campaign against illegal cartels

Kroes, Europe's top antitrust regulator, is leading a campaign against illegal cartels and has been chalking up record fines against companies targeted by her crackdown.

Page 94 of 169 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
After bumper  fines in 2006, EU regulators in January ordered 10 companies to pay 750 million euros for fixing prices and carving up the market in electrical generation equipment.
After the lift makers case, that  fine is now the third biggest ever against a  cartel.

Until Wednesday, the biggest  fine in a European cartel case was 855 million euros in penalties imposed on eight vitamin makers in 2001. The vitamin makers'  fine was later reduced to 790 million euros by an EU court.
Despite the record  fine on the lift makers, higher penalties are likely in future cases after the
commission tightened its rules for calculating  fines, making them heavier for repeat offenders and
long-lasting cartels. The reform could potentially cost guilty companies up to 10 times more than before.

The commission was also given a boost in its anti-cartel campaign earlier this month with a ruling from the European Court of Justice that gave regulators more freedom to fix  fines, especially how far back regulators can take previous offences into account.

HD               EU Fines 4 Elevator Makers EUR992 Million  For Price Fixing
WC              543 words
PD               21 February 2007
ET               12:25 PM
SN               Dow Jones News Service
SC               DJ
LA               English
CY               (c) 2007 Dow Jones & Company, Inc.

LP
BRUSSELS (Dow Jones)--The European  Commission on Wednesday fined four elevator and
escalator  makers a total of EUR992 million for fixing prices, Europe's largest-ever antitrust penalty.

ThyssenKrupp AG (TKA.XE) was fined EUR479.7 million, United Technologies Corp.'s (UTX)  Otis unit was fined EUR224.9 million, Schindler Holding AG (SCHN.EB) was hit with a EUR143.7 million penalty and  Kone was fined EUR142.1 million. [ 02-21-07 0642ET ]

TD
The sum of these  fines, which topped a EUR750 million penalty levied last month against makers of power-grid components, reflects the  commission's expanded powers to crack down on corporate cartels.

Under new antitrust guidelines established last year, the  commission can levy larger fines and apply an automatic 50% increase for repeat  cartel offenders. Since ThyssenKrupp had a previous cartel punishment - a EUR3.2 million fine in 1998 for fixing stainless steel prices - its  elevator penalty included this hike, bringing its fine close to the record EUR497 million Microsoft Corp. (MSFT) paid in 2004 for breaking monopoly laws.

The commission launched its investigation into the  elevator business in January 2004. Its probe showed that the four companies fixed prices and rigged bids in Belgium, Germany, the Netherlands and Luxembourg between 1995 and 2004.

The commission said Mitsubishi Elevator Europe BV, a division of Mitsubishi Electric Corp. (6503.TO), participated in the Dutch cartel and it fined the company EUR1.8 million.

"It is outrageous that the construction and maintenance costs of buildings, including hospitals, have been artificially bloated by these cartels. The national management of these companies knew what they were doing was wrong, but they tried to conceal their action and went ahead anyway," the commission's top antitrust official Neelie Kroes said in a statement. [ 02-21-07 0712ET ]

In all four countries, high-ranking managers held regular meetings related to the  cartel. The commission found evidence that the companies knew their behavior was illegal. The managers were careful to avoid detection, meeting in rural areas or abroad and using prepaid mobile phone cards to avoid tracking.

The commission said none of the companies contested these facts or other evidence unearthed in the investigation.

None of the companies qualified for the full immunity usually given to whistle-blowers.  Kone's penalty was reduced by about EUR138 million because it was the first to disclose the cartels in Belgium and Luxembourg and provided key evidence in the German investigation.  Otis' penalty was reduced by over EUR208 million because it disclosed the Dutch  cartel and cooperated with investigations in the other countries.

Finland-based Kone and Germany's ThyssenKrupp said they will examine the commission's decision before deciding whether to appeal. Spokesmen for Switzerland-based Schindler and the German unit of U.S.-based  Otis declined to comment.

Page 96 of 169 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
A key question in this case will be whether the companies' customers decide to sue. Over the past few years, the  commission has encouraged private damages claims to further deter  cartel offenders.
"Companies have the option of seeking damages" or renegotiating their contracts, said the
commission's antitrust spokesman Jonathan Todd.
-By Adam Cohen, Dow Jones Newswires; +322 741 1486; adam.cohen@dowjones.com [ 02-21-07
0820ET ]

HD                   ThyssenKrupp: FY07 Pretax Pft Target Unaffected  By EU Fine
WC                  288 words
PD                   21 February 2007
ET                    02:39 PM
SN                   Dow Jones News Service
SC                   DJ
LA                    English

LP
FRANKFURT (Dow Jones)--Industrial conglomerate ThyssenKrupp AG (TKA.XE) Wednesday said its pretax profit guidance for the fiscal year 2007 ending Oct. 31 will be unaffected by a EUR479.7 million  fine imposed by the European  Commission for price-fixing. [ 02-21-07
0855ET ]
However, the complete  fine will affect the company's bottom line, Chief Financial Officer Ulrich
Middelmann told analysts in a conference call.

TD
Middelmann said that the fine will be booked as other operating expenses and will be allocated in full to the company's elevator subsidiary.

Earlier this month, ThyssenKrupp raised its earnings and sales forecasts for fiscal year 2007, saying it expects to "significantly exceed" its sustainable pretax profit goal of EUR2.5 billion, after reaching EUR2.62 billion in fiscal year 2006.

In the conference call Wednesday, CFO Middelmann also said that ThyssenKrupp's current investment plan won't be affected by the E.U.  fine.

Earlier Wednesday, the E.U.  commission fined ThyssenKrupp and three other  elevator and escalator  makers a total of EUR992 million for fixing prices. The  fine is Europe's largest-ever antitrust penalty.

United Technologies Corp.'s (UTX)  Otis unit was fined EUR224.9 million,  Schindler Holding AG (SCHN.EB) was hit with a EUR143.7 million penalty and  Kone was find EUR142.1 million. [
02-21-07 0926ET ]

ThyssenKrupp earlier Wednesday said it will review the  fine, adding that it will then decide whether to appeal.
It said that it hasn't yet received official notice of the  fine. Company Web site:  http://www.thyssenkrupp.com
-By Jan Hromadko, Dow Jones Newswires; +49 69 29 725 503; jan.hromadko@dowjones.com [
02-21-07 0935ET ]

(Updates an item, published from around 1125 GMT, with company responses and further details.)

BRUSSELS (Dow Jones)--The European Commission on Wednesday levied Europe's largest-ever antitrust penalty, fining five  elevator and escalator  makers a total of EUR992 million for fixing prices.

TD
ThyssenKrupp AG (TKA.XE) was fined EUR479.7 million, United Technologies Corp.'s (UTX)  Otis unit was fined EUR224.9 million, Schindler Holding AG (SCHN.EB) was hit with a EUR143.7 million penalty and  Kone was fined EUR142.1 million.

The sum of these  fines, which topped a EUR790 million penalty levied in 2001 against makers of vitamin products, reflects the  Commission's expanded powers to crack down on corporate cartels.

Under new antitrust guidelines established last year, the  Commission can levy larger fines and apply an automatic 50% increase for repeat  cartel offenders. Since ThyssenKrupp had a previous cartel punishment - a EUR3.2 million  fine in 1998 for fixing stainless steel prices - its  elevator penalty included this added charge, bringing its  fine close to the record EUR497 million Microsoft Corp. (MSFT) paid in 2004 for breaking monopoly laws.

"Three million euros is lunch money," said Julian Joshua, a lawyer at the Howrey LLP law firm in Brussels and a former deputy head of the  commission's cartel team. Steeper  fines, such as the ones in the elevator case, "can have important financial consequences for companies," he added.

ThyssenKrupp shares fell about 2% to EUR38.60 after the  fine was disclosed. The other three companies had smaller dips in their share prices.
The Commission launched its investigation into the  elevatorand escalator  businesses in January
2004. Its probe showed that the four companies fixed prices and rigged bids in Belgium, Germany,
the Netherlands and Luxembourg between 1995 and 2004.

The Commission said Mitsubishi Elevator Europe BV, a division of Mitsubishi Electric Corp. (6503.TO), participated in the Dutch cartel and fined the company EUR1.8 million.

"It is outrageous that the construction and maintenance costs of buildings, including hospitals, have been artificially bloated by these cartels. The national management of these companies knew what they were doing was wrong, but they tried to conceal their action and went ahead anyway," the Commission's top antitrust official Neelie Kroes said in a statement.

In all four countries, high-ranking managers held regular meetings related to the  cartel. The commission found evidence that the companies knew their behavior was illegal. The managers were careful to avoid detection, meeting in rural areas or abroad and using prepaid mobile phone cards to avoid tracking. The  Commission said none of the companies contested these facts or other evidence unearthed in the investigation.

None of the companies qualified for the full immunity usually given to whistle-blowers.  Kone's penalty was reduced by about EUR138 million because it was the first to disclose the cartels in Belgium and Luxembourg and provided key evidence in the German investigation.  Otis' penalty was

Page 100 of 169 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
reduced by over EUR208 million because it disclosed the Dutch  cartel and cooperated with investigations in the other countries.

Otis said it will appeal the  Commission's decision at the European Court of First Instance in Luxembourg. Its parent company United Technologies said the  fine "will have no material impact" on its financial condition.

Schindler said it was "very surprised" at the size of the  fine since the companies were charged with local collusion, not running a pan-European  cartel.

Finland-based Kone and Germany's ThyssenKrupp said they will examine the  Commission's decision before deciding whether to appeal.

A key question in this case will be whether the companies' customers decide to sue. Over the past few years, the  Commission has encouraged private damages claims to further deter  cartel offenders.
"Companies have the option of seeking damages" or renegotiating their contracts, said the
Commission's antitrust spokesman Jonathan Todd.
-By Adam Cohen, Dow Jones Newswires; +322 741 1486; adam.cohen@dowjones.com [ 02-21-07
1131ET ]

Kone's penalty was reduced by about EUR138 million because it was the first to disclose the cartels in Belgium and Luxembourg and provided key evidence in the German investigation.  Otis' penalty was reduced by over EUR208 million because it disclosed the Dutch  cartel and cooperated with investigations in the other countries.

Finland-based Kone and Germany's ThyssenKrupp said they will examine the commission's decision before deciding whether to appeal. Spokesmen for Switzerland-based Schindler and the German unit of U.S.-based  Otis declined to comment.



Page 106 of 169 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
A key question in this case will be whether the companies' customers decide to sue. Over the past few years, the commission has encouraged private damages claims to further deter  cartel offenders.
"Companies have the option of seeking damages" or renegotiating their contracts, said the
commission's antitrust spokesman Jonathan Todd.

HD         UPDATE: EU Fines Elevator Makers EUR992M For Price-Fixing
WC        666 words
PD         21 February 2007
ET          05:31 PM
SN         Dow Jones International News
SC         DJI
LA          English
CY         (c) 2007 Dow Jones & Company, Inc.

LP
(Updates an item, published from around 1125 GMT, with company responses and further details.)

BRUSSELS (Dow Jones)--The European Commission on Wednesday levied Europe's largest-ever antitrust penalty, fining five  elevator and escalator  makers a total of EUR992 million for fixing prices.

TD
ThyssenKrupp AG (TKA.XE) was fined EUR479.7 million, United Technologies Corp.'s (UTX)  Otis unit was fined EUR224.9 million, Schindler Holding AG (SCHN.EB) was hit with a EUR143.7 million penalty and Kone was fined EUR142.1 million.

The sum of these  fines, which topped a EUR790 million penalty levied in 2001 against makers of vitamin products, reflects the  Commission's expanded powers to crack down on corporate cartels.

Under new antitrust guidelines established last year, the  Commission can levy larger fines and apply an automatic 50% increase for repeat  cartel offenders. Since ThyssenKrupp had a previous  cartel punishment - a EUR3.2 million  fine in 1998 for fixing stainless steel prices - its  elevator penalty
included this added charge, bringing its  fine close to the record EUR497 million Microsoft Corp. (MSFT)
paid in 2004 for breaking monopoly laws.

"Three million euros is lunch money," said Julian Joshua, a lawyer at the Howrey LLP law firm in Brussels and a former deputy head of the  commission's cartel team. Steeper  fines, such as the ones in the elevator case, "can have important financial consequences for companies," he added.

ThyssenKrupp shares fell about 2% to EUR38.60 after the  fine was disclosed. The other three companies had smaller dips in their share prices.
The Commission launched its investigation into the  elevatorand escalator  businesses in January
2004. Its probe showed that the four companies fixed prices and rigged bids in Belgium, Germany, the
Netherlands and Luxembourg between 1995 and 2004.

The Commission said Mitsubishi Elevator Europe BV, a division of Mitsubishi Electric Corp. (6503.TO), participated in the Dutch cartel and fined the company EUR1.8 million.

"It is outrageous that the construction and maintenance costs of buildings, including hospitals, have been artificially bloated by these cartels. The national management of these companies knew what they were doing was wrong, but they tried to conceal their action and went ahead anyway," the Commission's top antitrust official Neelie Kroes said in a statement.

In all four countries, high-ranking managers held regular meetings related to the  cartel. The commission found evidence that the companies knew their behavior was illegal. The managers were careful to avoid detection, meeting in rural areas or abroad and using prepaid mobile phone cards to avoid tracking. The  Commission said none of the companies contested these facts or other evidence unearthed in the investigation.

None of the companies qualified for the full immunity usually given to whistle-blowers.  Kone's penalty was reduced by about EUR138 million because it was the first to disclose the cartels in Belgium and Luxembourg and provided key evidence in the German investigation.  Otis' penalty was reduced by over EUR208 million because it disclosed the Dutch  cartel and cooperated with investigations in the other countries.

Page 108 of 169 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
Otis said it will appeal the  Commission's decision at the European Court of First Instance in Luxembourg. Its parent company United Technologies said the  fine "will have no material impact" on its financial condition.

Schindler said it was "very surprised" at the size of the  fine since the companies were charged with local collusion, not running a pan-European  cartel.

Finland-based Kone and Germany's ThyssenKrupp said they will examine the  Commission's decision before deciding whether to appeal.

A key question in this case will be whether the companies' customers decide to sue. Over the past few years, the Commission has encouraged private damages claims to further deter  cartel offenders.
"Companies have the option of seeking damages" or renegotiating their contracts, said the
Commission's antitrust spokesman Jonathan Todd.

HD           UPDATE: E.C. Fines Elevator Makers $1.3 Billion For Alleged  Cartel

By Steve Goldstein

LONDON (Dow Jones) -- The European  Commission on Wednesday doled out its largest-ever  fine for cartel behavior, $1.3 billion (992 million euros), to four companies for allegedly rigging prices to install and maintain  escalators and elevators.

TD
Those fined include the Otis unit of United Technologies (UTX) , and Germany's ThyssenKrupp, which received the harshest fine for being a repeat offender.

The E.C. said the companies rigged bids for procurement contracts, fixed prices and allocated projects to each other, shared markets, and exchanged commercially important and confidential information in Belgium, Germany, Luxembourg and the Netherlands.
They did so from between at least 1995 and 2004, the  commission alleged.

"It is outrageous that the construction and maintenance costs of buildings, including hospitals, have been artificially bloated by these cartels," Competition Commissioner Neelie Kroes said in a statement.

In all four cartels, high-ranking national management participated in regular meetings and discussions and did so in a James Bond-esque way, the E.C. alleged -- they usually met in bars and restaurants, they traveled to the countryside or even abroad, and they used pre-paid mobile phone cards to avoid tracking.
None of the firms contested the facts, the E.C. said.

Otis was the first to provide the E.C. with information on the alleged  cartel in the Netherlands, and Finland's  Kone was the first to provide the commission with information on the cartels in Belgium and Luxembourg; as a result, neither firm was fined for activities in the markets for which they provided information.
ThyssenKrupp received the largest fine, 480 million euros.  Otis was fined 225 million euros,  Kone
was fined 142 million euros and Schindler was fined 144 million euros.

ThyssenKrupp shares weakened 2.5%,  Schindler shares rose 3.1% in Switzerland and  Kone was largely unmoved. United Technologies has yet to trade. [ 02-21-07 0816ET ]

ThyssenKrupp, Schindler, Kone and Otis - the world's biggest lift manufacturers - will today be punished and fined by the European Commission for operating a  price-fixing cartel.

The European Union antitrust watchdog issued formal charges against the four groups in October2005, and according to several people familiar with the case will hand down its final ruling at a meeting in Brussels tomorrow.

TD

HD          EC Fines Lift Makers 992 Mln Euro for Cartel Participation
WC         278 words
PD          21 February 2007
SN          German News Digest
SC          GERMND
LA          English
CY          (c) 2007 AII Data Processing Ltd. All Rights Reserved.

LP
The European  Commission (EC) has imposed a 992 mln euro ($1.303 bln)  fine on four of the world's largest lift makers, including German steel and engineering group ThyssenKrupp AG, for  cartel participation, European Commissioner for Competition Neelie Kroes said on February 21, 2007.

ThyssenKrupp alone will be  fined with a 479 mln euro ($629.62 mln), the largest  fine EC has ever imposed on a single company for  cartel violations.

TD
The fine of the German company has been raised by 50 pct, as ThyssenKrupp was a repeat offender, EC said. In 1998, the company was  fined on grounds of stainless steel  cartel participation.
The other  cartel participants are U.S. United Technologies Corp's  Otis, fined with 225 mln euro
($295.75 mln), Swiss  Schindler AG, with 143.7 mln euro ($188.88 mln), and Finnish  Kone Oyj, with
142.1 mln euro ($186.78 mln).

Kone and Otis will receive milder fines, as the companies are working in collaboration with the EC
investigators.

According to the EC, the companies were involved in  price-fixing for the installation and maintenance of lifts and  escalators in Belgium, Germany, Luxembourg and the Netherlands between 1995 and
2004.

The cartel participants rigged bids for procurement contracts, fixed prices and allocated projects to each other, shared markets and exchanged commercially important and confidential information, the Commission said.

The construction and maintenance costs of buildings, including hospitals, have been artificially boosted by the cartels, Kroes said.
The effects of the cartels are to be felt in the next years as well, EC said.

HD     (EU) EU/COMPETITION: Commission fines members of lifts and escalators cartels over €990 million
WC    751 words
PD     22 February 2007
SN     Agence Europe
SC     AGEU
LA      English
CY     Not Available for Re-Dissemination, except as permitted by your subscriber agreement. (c) Agence
Europe, Brussels 2007. All rights reserved.

LP
Brussels, 21/02/2007 (Agence Europe) - Following the toughening up of the anti-trust measures adopted in July 2006, which saw a total of €1.8bn in  fines, the Commission announced on Wednesday 21
February that a series of  fines would be imposed for illegal agreements in 2007. The €992,312,200 in fines affect four companies involved in the installation and maintenance of lifts and escalators Otis (owned by US United Technologies), ThyssenKrupp (Germany),  Schindler (Switzerland) and KONE (Finland), which were dividing up the markets in Belgium, Germany, Luxembourg and the Netherlands between 1995-2005. These  fines are the highest ever imposed by the  Commission.

TD
Competition Commissioner Neelie Kroes said: "It is outrageous that the construction and maintenance costs of buildings, including hospitals, have been artificially bloated by these cartels. The national management of these companies knew that what they were doing was wrong, but they tried to conceal their action and went ahead anyway”.

The Commission started the investigation on its own initiative using information brought to its attention. This led to surprise inspections in January 2004 at the premises of lift and  escalator  manufacturers throughout Europe. In turn, these inspections triggered many applications from the companies for immunity or reduction of  fines under the Commission's 2002 Leniency Notice.

The evidence uncovered in the inspections showed that the companies ran illegal cartels in Belgium, Germany, Luxembourg and the Netherlands. This was further confirmed by numerous documents and corporate statements appealing for immunity.

The companies allocated tenders and other contracts for the sale, installation, maintenance and modernisation of lifts and  escalators with the aim of freezing market shares and fixing prices. Business secrets and confidential information on bidding patterns and prices between the  cartel participants were also exchanged. Projects that were rigged included lifts and  escalators for hospitals, railway stations, shopping centres and commercial buildings.

The allocation of projects was similar in all four member states. The companies informed each other of calls for tender, and co-ordinated their bids according to their pre-agreed  cartel quotas. Fake bids, too high to be accepted, were lodged by the companies who were not supposed to win the tender, in order to give the impression of genuine competition. The companies kept and circulated amongst themselves updated project lists for Belgium, Germany and Luxembourg. In Germany and the Netherlands, it was often agreed that the company that had a longstanding or good relationship with a particular customer should secure most of that customer's contracts; referred to by the companies as the "existing customers remain" principle.

In all four cartels high-ranking national management (such as managing directors, sales and services directors and heads of customer service departments) participated in regular meetings and discussions. There is evidence that the companies were aware that their behaviour was illegal and they took care to avoid detection; they usually met in bars and restaurants, they travelled to the countryside or even abroad, and they used pre-paid mobile phone cards to avoid tracking.

The fines reflect not just the severity of the offence but also the size of the markets in question. The companies involved are responsible for almost the entire supply and maintenance of lifts and  escalators in the countries in question, essential materials for the effective functioning of almost all kinds of institution.  Otis itself explained that the equivalent of the total world population uses its lifts, mechanical
Page 114 of 169 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
escalators and moving pathways every nine days. Ms Kroes also drew attention to the fact that public institutions, such as hospitals had been affected by the excessive costs of these cartels.

The situation was further worsened by the fact that ThyssenKrupp companies were repeat offenders. Part of the group had already been charged for creating illegal cartels (EUROPE 9332) and all of its subsidiaries were involved in the cases being investigated, leading to a 50% increase in the  fines
imposed on them, in compliance with last June's enhanced anti-trust sanctions (EUROPE 9221). Among the 18 subsidiaries under investigation, only certain Kone subsidiaries and one Otis subsidiary benefited from clemency for having cooperated with the Commission in the required time frame.

The accused did not oppose the statement of objections or call for an oral hearing. The day the Commission's announcement was made ThyssenKrupp,  Schindler and Kone indicated that they would wait for an analysis of the  Commission's investigation before deciding on what approach they would take. (cd)

"JP Morgan" (Nick Paton): Following the margin disappointment in 1H 06, our estimates look for the company to miss its 10% margin target in the E&E division with a 9.8% margin in the 2H, after an 8.6% margin in the 1H. The companys focus on new equipment will, we believe, put downward pressure on the margin which investors are unlikely to give the company credit for in the short term. We believe  Schindler will seek a structural exit from its ALSO business but that it needs to get greater scale before doing so. We believe ALSO needs revenues of Sfr8bn, a significant increase on our 2006 estimate of Sfr2.9bn. We believe the company has to increase its exposure to this unappealing business before an exit. "Neutral"

"ZKB" (Martin Hüsler): Nebst den Zahlen 2006 richten wir unser Augenmerk auf den Ausblick für 2007 und auf die strategische Weiterentwicklung der Logistiktochter ALSO. 2006 dürfte wachstumsbedingt einen Margenrückgang gebracht haben, da das profitablere Servicegeschäft weniger dynamisch gewachsen ist. "Vontobel" (Serge Rotzer): We will be interested in learning about the progress made in H2 06 regarding the implementation of the new growth strategy. The motto in H1 06 was 'growth before margins', leading Schindler to post stronger organic growth than its main competitors (13% vs. 10% for  Kone and ~5% for Otis). The price to be paid for this strategy was a temporary fall in the margin from 9.2% to 8.6% -  Otis
and Kone were both able to increase their margins during the same period. We will also be keen to get an assessment from management concerning a possible  fine in connection with the ongoing EU
investigation. "Market Perform"

Demand for new installations should also remain solid owing to the global launch last year of Schindlers innovative E+E product portfolio. BY      David Charter

LP
BRUSSELS

BRUSSELS - The penalty for being caught running a cartel in Europe increased dramatically yesterday when a group of elevator companies was hit with record fines totalling $1.3 billion U.S.

TD
Managers from the "big four"  elevator providers -- Otis, Schindler, ThyssenKrupp and  Kone -- were caught rigging contracts in four European countries.

Deals to install and maintain  elevators  for hospitals, shopping centres and commercial buildings were carved up between the businesses, the European  Commission found.

The companies even colluded over new  elevators  for Commission headquarters in Brussels, as well as for the European Court of Justice in Luxembourg, officials said yesterday.

The fine beats a $975 million penalty imposed last month on 11 firms in a  cartel for gas-insulated switchgear products.

It showed that Neelie Kroes, the Competition Commissioner, meant business when she vowed last year to use more of power available to her.
She can fine a company up to 10 per cent of its turnover for  cartel activity.

Ms. Kroes said that managers of the  elevator companies met in bars away from main cities and, to avoid detection, even used pay-as-you-go mobile phones when deciding whose "turn" it was to win a contract.

"National management of these companies knew what they were doing was wrong, but they tried to conceal their action and went ahead, anyway,'' Ms. Kroes said.

''The damage caused by this  cartel will last for many years because it covered not only the initial supply, but also the subsequent maintenance of  elevators  and other lift devices.
For these companies, the memory of this  fine should last just as long."
ThyssenKrupp, branded a repeat offender because the German group was found to be in a steel  cartel in
1998, was given the largest antitrust  fine for one company so far, of about $623 million.
It said that it may fight the amount of the  fine, as did Finland's Kone, fined $185 million. Otis, part of the
American UTC, was  fined $291 million and the Swiss company Schindler $186 million.
Misubishi Elevator Europe was fined $2.34 million for its role in the Netherlands.

ART   Photo: Ville Myllynen/AFP/Getty Images / Up to No Good: Businessmen take a ride at  Kone headquarters in Espoo, Finland. The Finnish  elevator company -- along with  Otis, Schindler, ThyssenKrupp -- were fined a record $1.3 billion U.S. yesterday.

Page 123 of 169 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.


HD        FT.com site : Brussels imposes  992m fine on lift cartel. BY         Tobias Buck in Brussels
WC       512 words
PD        22 February 2007
SN        Financial Times (FT.Com)
SC        FTCOM
LA         English
CY        (c) 2007 The Financial Times Limited. All rights reserved

LP
The European Union's top antitrust regulator on Wednesday slapped a record 992m  fine on ThyssenKrupp and four other lift manufacturers, in the latest sign that the Brussels watchdog is drastically ramping up the financial pain for companies that violate competition rules.

It was the highest penalty imposed by the European Commission, topping a previous record set only last month. Brussels hopes that the recent increase in antitrust fines will deter companies from abusing the law and encourage people involved in cartels to blow the whistle on co-conspirators in exchange for immunity.

TD
Neelie Kroes, the EU competition commissioner and the -official behind Brussels' get-tough approach, said the lift and  escalator cartel had behaved "outrageously", while her spokesman said taxpayers, public authorities and other customers had been "ripped off big time" by the  cartel.

ThyssenKrupp, the German engineering group, received the toughest penalty,? 479.7m ($630m, GBP322.5m).  Otis, a unit of US group United Technologies, was  fined 224.9m, Schindler of Switzerland was given a 143.7m penalty and Finland's  Kone was ordered to pay 142.1m. Mitsubishi's Dutch subsidiary was  fined 1.8m.

Ms Kroes said: "The damage caused by this  cartel will last for many years because it covered not only the initial supply but also the subsequent maintenance of lifts and  elevators.  For these companies the memory of this  fine should last just as long."

The Commission said the lifts installed in its own headquarters as well as at the European Court of Justice in Luxembourg were covered by the  cartel. It urged customers of the companies involved in the agreement to renegotiate their contracts, and said they might have the right to sue for damages.
John Pheasant, a competition law specialist and partner at law firm Hogan?&?Hartson, said yesterday's
fines were part of a wider trend. "In terms of  fines, the Commission is now the toughest authority in
the world. The trend towards higher  fines started a few years back, but recently there has been a
distinct acceleration."

The Commission last year passed new fining guidelines that will allow Brussels to further increase the financial pain on abusive companies in all new cases. Officials have argued that the new rules will mean that more companies will be hit with the maximum penalty - a  fine equivalent to 10 per cent of their annual global turnover.

The lift  cartel operated in Germany, the Netherlands, Belgium and Luxembourg between at least 1995 and 2004. According to the  Commission, the groups co-ordinated their bids to ensure that a designated group would win specific contracts.

"[To agree on bids] they usually met in bars and restaurants, they travelled to the countryside or even abroad, and they used pre-paid mobile cards to avoid tracking," the  Commission said.

ThyssenKrupp, United Technologies,  Schindler and Kone said they were studying the Commission ruling, with  Schindler adding it was "very surprised" by the high  fines. United Technologies said Otis would appeal against the decision.

HD         Roundup: EU imposes  record billion-euro fine on lift cartels
WC        498 words
PD         22 February 2007
SN         Xinhua News Agency
SC         XNEWS
LA         English
CY         (c) Copyright 2007 Xinhua News Agency

LP
BRUSSELS, Feb. 21 (Xinhua) -- The European  Commission on Wednesday handed down a record antitrust  fine of almost a billion euros over several lift companies for running illegal cartels.

The lift and  elevator makers Otis, Kone, Schindler and ThyssenKrupp groups were given a combined penalty worth 992 million euros, the biggest ever fine imposed by the European Union's antitrust watchdog for  cartel violations.

TD
Those major lift companies was accused of operating cartels for the installation and maintenance of lifts and escalators in Belgium, Germany, Luxembourg and the Netherlands between 1995 and 2004.

The European  Commission conducted surprise inspections in January 2004 at the premises of those companies throughout Europe after receiving a tip-off about the  cartel.

The three-year investigation revealed that they rigged bids for procurement contracts, fixed prices and allocated projects to each other, shared markets and exchanged commercially important and confidential information.

Even the European Commission's headquarters in Brussels and the EU courts building in Luxembourg were affected by their cartel arrangement.

The effects of this  cartel may continue for twenty to fifty years as maintenance is often done by the companies that installed the equipment in the first place, and by cartelizing the installation, the companies distorted the markets for years to come, the  Commission said.

Among those companies, ThyssenKrupp alone had to pay almost half of the total  fine, the heaviest ever imposed by the Commission on a single company in a cartel case. The German group's  fine was increased by 50 percent to 480 million euros because it was a repeated offender.

Otis, a unit of US conglomerate United Technologies Corp., received a  fine of 225 million euros, followed by Switzerland-based  Schindler with a penalty of 144 million euros and  Kone of Finland with
142 million euros.
Japanese company Mitsubishi was also  fined 1.8 million euros for its role limited to the Netherlands. "The damage caused by this  cartel will last for many years because it covered not only the initial supply
but also the subsequent maintenance of lifts and  elevators  - for these companies the memory of this
fine should last just as long." the EU Competition Commissioner Neelie Kroes said.

The United Technologies Corp. said in a statement that it felt disappointed with the  Commission's decision and will appeal to the European Court of First Instance. Nine  Otis employees in Europe have since been identified and dismissed for breaking the company's code of ethics.
ThyssenKrupp also said it was considering whether to appeal against the decision.

The European  Commission imposed a total  fine of 855 million euros on eight vitamin makers in 2001, the biggest penalty in an EU  cartel case before Wednesday's decision. The  fine was later reduced to
790 million euros by an EU court.


Page 126 of 169 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
Almost a month ago, the  Commission ordered ten companies to pay 750 million euros for fixing prices and carving up the market in gas insulated switchgear.
