SE            Other World Stories
HD            EU fines lift companies €992m for role in cartel. BY            Jamie Smyth in Brussels
WC           478 words
PD            22 February 2007
SN            Irish Times
SC            IRTI
LA            English
CY            (c) 2007, The Irish Times.

LP
EU: The European  Commission has levied a record €992 million  fine on several lift manufacturers for illegally rigging bids and fixing prices.

The EU executive said yesterday the German firm ThyssenKrupp was given the highest penalty awarded to date in a  cartel case, €480 million, because it was a repeat offender.

TD
Four other companies were also handed multimillion euro  fines.
The commission investigation uncovered evidence that lift manufacturers had operated an illegal
cartel in Belgium, Germany, Luxembourg and the Netherlands between 1995 and 2004.

It also admitted that it had fallen prey to the  cartel by purchasing lifts and maintenance contracts for its own headquarters, the Berlaymont.

The European Court of Justice (ECJ) in Luxembourg had also been ripped off by the lift manufacturer's  cartel. Hospitals, public authorities and private firms were also victims of the scam, according to the results of the  commission's cartel investigation initiated several years ago.

"The result of this  cartel is that taxpayers, public authorities and property developers have been ripped off big time," said Jonathan Todd, spokesman for competition commissioner Neelie Kroes. "Its effects will be felt for the next 20-50 years because a lot of these companies make their money from the maintenance which is done following the installation of the  escalators and elevators."

The commission said that companies that had purchased maintenance contracts from the lift firms involved in the cartel could go to court to renegotiate their agreements.

Aside from ThyssenKrupp, the other parent companies implicated in the  cartel are: Otis, which is owned by US firm United Technologies, the Finnish firm Kone, Schildler of Switzerland and the Japanese firm Mitsubishi Electric Corporation. The commission fined Otis €225 million, Kone €142 million, Schindler €144 million and Mitsubishi €1.8 million. Seventeen different subsidiary firms took part in the cartel.

The investigation by the commission in the four member states concerned showed that bids were allocated in similar ways by the cartel. The companies informed each other of calls for tender and co-ordinated their bids according to their pre-agreed  cartel quotas.

Fake bids, too high to be accepted, were lodged by the companies who were not supposed to win the tender, in order to give the impression of genuine competition.

The companies kept and circulated amongst themselves updated project lists for Belgium, Germany and Luxembourg.

In all four cartels high-ranking national management (such as managing directors, sales and services directors and heads of customer service departments) participated in regular meetings and discussions.



Page 129 of 169 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
There is evidence that the companies were aware that their behaviour was illegal and they took care to avoid detection; they usually met in bars and restaurants, they travelled to the countryside or even abroad, and they used pre-paid mobile phone cards to avoid tracking.

HD           EU Fines Companies  for Price-Fixing
BY            By Adam Cohen
WC           194 words
PD            22 February 2007
SN            The Wall Street Journal
SC            J
PG           C12
LA            English
CY            (Copyright (c) 2007, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

LP
BRUSSELS -- A record 992 million euros ($1.3 billion)  fine imposed on five elevator and escalator makers for fixing prices shows European Union regulators are intent on using their expanded power to crack down on cartels.
ThyssenKrupp AG was fined 479.7 million euros, United Technologies Corp.'s  Otis unit was fined
224.9 million euros, Schindler Holding AG was hit with a 143.7 million euros penalty and Kone Corp.
was fined 142.1 million euros. The European  Commission also said Mitsubishi  Elevator Europe BV,
a division of Mitsubishi Electric Corp., participated in the Dutch  cartel and fined the company 1.8
million euros.

TD
Under antitrust guidelines established last year, the  commission can levy larger fines and apply an automatic 50% increase for repeat  cartel offenders. Because ThyssenKrupp had a previous  cartel punishment, its  elevator penalty included this added charge.

The commission launched its investigation in January 2004. Its probe showed the companies fixed prices and rigged bids in Belgium, Germany, the Netherlands and Luxembourg between 1995 and
2004.

Brussels, Feb. 21 (Jiji Press)--The European Commission on Wednesday imposed a total of 992.31 million euros in fines on four U.S. and European elevator makers and a European unit of Japan's Mitsubishi Electric Corp. for forming price cartels for elevators  and escalators.
The four firms are industry leader  Otis of the United States,  Kone of Finland,  Schindler of
Switzerland and ThyssenKrupp of Germany.

TD
The total amount of  fines is the biggest for a price  cartel case, according to the  commission, the executive arm of the European Union.
Mitsubishi Elevator Europe BV, the Mitsubishi Electric unit in the Netherlands, was slapped with
fines of 1.84 million euros.

ThyssenKrupp was  fined 479.66 million euros, the biggest penalty imposed ever on any single company.
According to the  commission, the cartels were operated in Belgium, Germany, Luxembourg and the
Netherlands between at least 1995 and 2004 in a clear violation of its treaty rules.
Mitsubishi Elevator Europe was accused of having participated in the  cartel in the Netherlands. The commission said the companies "rigged bids for procurement contracts, fixed prices and
allocated projects to each other." They also "shared markets, and exchanged commercially important
and confidential information," it said.

The effects of the cartels may continue for 20 to 50 years as maintenance work is often done by the companies that installed the equipment, the  commission said. "By cartelizing the installation, the companies distorted the markets for years to come," it said.

ZURICH, Feb 22 (Reuters) - Swiss  elevator and escalator  maker Schindler is seen posting a 29 percent rise in full-year net income to 518 million francs, according to the average in a Reuters poll.

Schindler has said it would appeal against a European  Commission decision to fine it 144 million euros
($189.2 million) for  price fixing.  The fine will not affect 2006 results, the firm said.

Sales at the world's second-largest lift and  escalator  maker are expected to rise 24 percent to 10.98 billion francs, according to a poll of nine analysts.
Sales of  elevators  and escalators are expected to rise 14 percent to 7.875 billion francs.

HD               5 Elevator Makers Are Fined In Europe in Pricing  Case
CR               AP
WC               326 words
PD                22 February 2007
SN                The New York Times
SC                NYTF
LA                English
CY                Copyright 2007 The New York Times Company. All Rights Reserved.

LP
BRUSSELS, Feb. 21 -- European Union regulators on Wednesday  fined Otis Elevator and four other elevator makers $1.3 billion for operating cartels for the installation and maintenance of elevators  and escalators.
The union's competition commissioner, Neelie Kroes, said the costs of buildings and hospitals were
''artificially bloated by these cartels.''

TD
Jonathan Todd, a union spokesman, said the  fines represented the ''largest ever'' for  price fixing
in the European Union.

The authorities said the  cartel operated from at least 1995 until 2004 in Germany, Belgium, Luxembourg and the Netherlands.

The companies fined were Otis, a unit of United Technologies of Hartford; the German conglomerate ThyssenKrupp; Kone of Finland;  Schindler Holding of Switzerland; and subsidiaries of Mitsubishi  Elevator Europe, a unit of Mitsubishi Electric of Japan.

George David, the chief executive of United Technologies, said in a statement that company officials were ''disappointed by the conduct revealed through the  commission's and U.T.C.'s own investigations.''
He said nine Otis employees were dismissed over the matter.

Otis and United Technologies disclosed the investigation in 2004 and have cooperated with the European Commission, Mr. David said. Nevertheless, he said,  Otis will appeal the decision to a European court.

Otis was fined 225 million euros ($296 million).

ThyssenKrupp was given the largest  fine, just over 479 million euros, because the company was labeled a ''repeat offender'' by regulators, Mr. Todd said.

ThyssenKrupp, which is based in Frankfurt, said it would review the  fine and then decide whether to appeal.

Schindler was fined 144 million euros;  Kone, 142 million euros; and Mitsubishi's Dutch subsidiary,
1.8 million euros.

Schindler and Kone said they also would review the decision before deciding whether to appeal.

fine because it was labeled a ''repeat offender.'' (Photo by Martin Meissner/Associated Press)

HD     EU slaps $1.3 billion cartel fine on United Technologies' Otis unit, 4 other elevator makers

BY      By CONSTANT BRAND CR     Associated Press Writer WC    932 words
PD     22 February 2007
ET      04:19 AM
SN     Associated Press Newswires
SC     APRS
LA      English
CY     (c) 2007. The Associated Press. All Rights Reserved.

LP
BRUSSELS, Belgium (AP) - European Union regulators on Wednesday  fined United Technologies'  Otis unit and four other elevator makers $1.3 billion for operating cartels for the installation and maintenance of elevators  and escalators in Germany, Belgium, Luxembourg and the Netherlands.

EU spokesman Jonathan Todd said the  fines represented the "largest ever" for  price fixing  in the European Union, and EU Competition Commissioner Neelie Kroes said the costs of buildings and hospitals were "artificially bloated by these cartels."

TD
"The result of this  cartel is that taxpayers, public authorities and property developers have been ripped off big time," Todd told reporters. "The companies ensured that by rigging the bids and sharing the markets that the prices paid for the installation and the maintenance were way above what they would have been if there had been a competitive market."

Fined were Hartford, Conn.-based United Technologies Corp., the corporate parent of Otis Elevator; German conglomerate ThyssenKrupp AG; Finland's  Kone Corp.; Switzerland's Schindler Holding AG; and subsidiaries of Japan's Mitsubishi  Elevator Europe BV.

George David, chairman and chief executive of United Technologies, said in a statement that company officials are "disappointed by the conduct revealed through the  Commission's and UTC's own investigations."

Otis was fined 225 million euros ($295.8 million).

"UTC will not tolerate unethical behaviors of any kind," he said. "The nine local  Otis employees who violated our code of ethics and regulatory compliance programs were quickly identified and dismissed and we have reinvigorated our ethics and compliance programs worldwide."

Otis and United Technologies disclosed the investigation in 2004 and have cooperated with the European Commission, David said.  Otis and UTC have received full or partial reductions in penalties for its cooperation and the outcome will have "no material impact" on UTC's financial condition or  Otis' competitive position.

United Technologies is waiting for the full text of the decision and will review it before reporting on its impact on its 2007 earnings guidance. In addition, David said  Otis will appeal the decision to a European court.

ThyssenKrupp was given the largest  fine, just over 479 million euros ($630 million), because the company was labeled a "repeat offender" by EU regulators, Todd said.
Frankfurt, Germany-based ThyssenKrupp said it will review the  fine and then decide whether to appeal.

Schindler was fined 144 million euros ($189.3 million),  Kone 142 million euros ($187 million) and
Mitsubishi's Dutch subsidiary was  fined 1.8 million euros ($2.37 million).


Page 138 of 169 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
Mitsubishi Elevator Europe has received notice of the  fine and will decide how to respond after reviewing the details, parent company Mitsubishi Electric Corp. said in a statement. Tokyo-based Mitsubishi Electric holds a 51 percent stake in the European  elevator maker; Dutch construction company TBI Holdings BV owns the remainder.

The European  Commission said the cartel had worked to rig bids for procurement contracts, fix prices and share markets "between at least 1995 and 2004." It said the companies "did not contest the facts" found by EU regulators, adding none of the accused requested a hearing to answer the allegations.

But Schindler said the company was "very surprised at the size of the  fine," because it claimed the EU had found "no evidence of pan-European collusion among companies in the European  elevator industry." It said in a statement that it had not yet decided whether it would appeal.

Kone also said it will "examine the  commission's decision and decide on potential action."

Wednesday's fine tops a 750 million euro ($978 million)  fine imposed last month against 10 companies for running a cartel to fix prices for heavy equipment used by power utilities, reflecting stepped-up action by Kroes against price fixing.

She has vowed a crackdown under new antitrust guidelines agreed to last year that allow EU regulators to come down harder on repeat offenders like ThyssenKrupp, which faces an automatic 50 percent increase for repeated  price-fixing activities.

The German company had already received a previous  cartel fine, worth 3.2 million euros ($4.2 million) in 1998 for fixing stainless steel prices. Wednesday's punishment brings ThyssenKrupp close to the 479 million euros fine paid by U.S. software giant Microsoft Corp. for breaking European monopoly laws.

EU regulators found evidence from "numerous documents and corporate statements" to prove the companies allocated tenders and contracts for the sale, installation and maintenance of  elevators  "with the aim of freezing market shares and fixing prices," the  commission said.

"Projects that were rigged included lifts and  escalators for hospitals, railway stations, shopping centers and commercial buildings," it said, adding such acts were decided upon between managing and service directors during regular meetings between  cartel members.

"There is evidence that the companies were aware that their behavior was illegal and they took care to avoid detection," the  commission said, adding that the meetings usually took place in bars and restaurants or in secret locations outside cities to avoid suspicion.
EU officials said they even used prepaid mobile phone cards to avoid tracking.

"The national management of these companies know what they were doing was wrong, but they tried to conceal their action and went ahead anyway," Kroes said in a statement. "The damage caused by this cartel will last for many years because it covered not only the initial supply but also the subsequent maintenance of lifts and  elevators."

The EU conducted surprise raids against the companies in January 2004 after EU regulators were tipped off.

WSJ(2/22) EU  Fines Companies For Price-Fixing
(From THE WALL STREET JOURNAL) By Adam Cohen

TD
BRUSSELS -- A record 992 million euros ($1.3 billion)  fine imposed on five elevator and escalator makers for fixing prices shows European Union regulators are intent on using their expanded power to crack down on cartels.
ThyssenKrupp AG was fined 479.7 million euros, United Technologies Corp.'s  Otis unit was fined
224.9 million euros, Schindler Holding AG was hit with a 143.7 million euros penalty and Kone Corp.
was fined 142.1 million euros. The European  Commission also said Mitsubishi  Elevator Europe BV, a
division of Mitsubishi Electric Corp., participated in the Dutch  cartel and fined the company 1.8 million
euros.

Under antitrust guidelines established last year, the  commission can levy larger fines and apply an automatic 50% increase for repeat  cartel offenders. Because ThyssenKrupp had a previous  cartel punishment, its  elevator penalty included this added charge.

The commission launched its investigation in January 2004. Its probe showed the companies fixed prices and rigged bids in Belgium, Germany, the Netherlands and Luxembourg between 1995 and
2004.
---
John W. Miller contributed to this article.

HD      CARTELS : LIFT CARTEL: LARGEST FINES EVER WC     495 words
PD      22 February 2007
SN      Europolitics SC      EURREP LA      English
CY      Copyright 2007 Europe Information Service All Rights Reserved

LP
On 21 February, the European  Commission announced swingeing fines totalling over €992 million against 17 companies for involvement in a series of cartels for the installation and maintenance of lifts and escalators. It also admitted that as well as being prosecutor, the  Commission was itself a victim:
the lifts in the Commission's Brussels headquarters, the Berlaymont building - and those in the European
Court complex in Luxembourg - were installed by companies involved in the  cartel.

The illegal agreements covered Belgium, Germany, Luxembourg and the Netherlands, involving four major company groupings:  KONE - fined €142,120,000;  Otis - fined €224,932,950;  Schindler -
€143,748,000 and ThyssenKrupp. The fine against ThyssenKrupp was increased by 50% for previous cartel activity resulting in a €479,669,850 penalty - the largest ever against one group of companies. Another single firm involved was Mitsubishi  Elevator Europe, which participated in the Dutch  cartel: it was fined €1,841,400.

TD
Over at least five years from 1995 to 2004, senior management of the firms travelled regularly to secret locations to discuss  cartel arrangements. The  Commission investigation, which was not prompted by a whistleblower, began with surprise inspections in January 2004. These set off a flurry of immunity applications, which alerted the Commission to the fact that the  cartel was also active in Belgium and Luxembourg. Some  KONE subsidiaries and Otis Netherlands were granted full immunity for supplying this information.

The fact that many of the contracts were public and subject to tenders shows just how bold the  cartel was. Not content with fixing prices, sharing markets and exchanging confidential information, the companies involved in the  cartel blatantly rigged bids for major public procurement contracts. They circulated information on calls for tender and took turns to win'. The firm with its name at the top of the rota would submit a bid and all the others would ensure their bids were higher: the apparent competitive process was therefore a sham.

The level of the  fines reflects the size of the companies and the value of the markets. In the words of Commission spokesperson Jonathan Todd, the  cartel resulted in taxpayers, public authorities and property developers being "ripped off big time". "Prices were way above what they would have been otherwise," he said. The effects of the  cartel will continue to be felt for the next 20-50 years, because the contracts concerned both installation and maintenance. Competition Commissioner Neelie Kroes commented, "the memory of these  fines should last just as long."

Todd went on to explain that companies or public bodies affected by the  cartel could use the Commission decision as grounds for renegotiating their contracts (in some member states they may have a legal right to do so) or could invoke the  Commission decision if they chose to take action for
damages in national courts. He declined to say, however, whether the  Commission itself would be taking the lead and going down this legal avenue.

HD     Financial  Times Deutschland: ThyssenKrupp receives  fine from European Commission
(Aufzugfirmen sollen 1 Mrd. Euro Strafe zahlen).
WC    193 words
PD     22 February 2007
SN     Financial Times Deutschland
SC     FTDEFT
LA      English
CY     The Financial Times Limited. European Intelligence Wire. All material subject to copyright. Financial
Times Deutschland (c) 2007 All rights reserved.

LP
The European  Commission has imposed a record fine of 992m euros on five manufacturers of lifts and escalators, including German steel and engineering group ThyssenKrupp. The companies have been accused of entering into illegal price agreements in Germany, Belgium, the Netherlands and Luxembourg between 1995 and 2004; they are also said to have obtained contracts for each other and thus to have divided up the market among themselves. ThyssenKrupp alone will have to pay 479.7m euros, the highest ever individual fine imposed by the EU.

As the company has not made any reserves for the  fine on its balance sheet, the  fine will place a burden on ThyssenKrupp's accounts. It is also thought that customers could contest maintenance contracts with the company. ThyssenKrupp is examining whether to appeal against the  fine. The other companies in the cartel were US group Otis, Swiss company  Schindler, Finnish manufacturer  Kone and Mitsubishi Elevator Europe, which is based in the Netherlands.

TD

SE       INTERNATIONAL NEWS
HD       Brussels imposes  Euros 992m fine on lift cartel. BY       By TOBIAS BUCK
WC      518 words
PD       22 February 2007
SN       Financial Times
SC       FTFT
ED       London Ed1
PG       Page 8
LA        English
CY       (c) 2007 The Financial Times Limited. All rights reserved

LP
The European Union's top antitrust regulator yesterday slapped a record Euros 992m  fine on ThyssenKrupp and four other lift manufacturers, in the latest sign that the Brussels watchdog is drastically ramping up the financial pain for companies that violate competition rules.

It was the highest penalty imposed by the European Commission, topping a previous record set only last month. Brussels hopes that the recent increase in antitrust fines will deter companies from abusing the law and encourage people involved in cartels to blow the whistle on co-conspirators in exchange for immunity.

TD
Neelie Kroes, the EU competition commissioner and the -official behind Brussels' get-tough approach, said the lift and  escalator cartel had behaved "outrageously", while her spokesman said taxpayers, public authorities and other customers had been "ripped off big time" by the  cartel.
ThyssenKrupp, the German engineering group, received the toughest penalty, Euros 479.7m (Dollars
630m, Pounds 322.5m).  Otis, a unit of US group United Technologies, was fined Euros 224.9m,
Schindler of Switzerland was given a Euros 143.7m penalty and Finland's Kone was ordered to pay
Euros 142.1m. Mitsubishi's Dutch subsidiary was  fined Euros 1.8m.

Ms Kroes said: "The damage caused by this  cartel will last for many years because it covered not only the initial supply but also the subsequent maintenance of lifts and  elevators.  For these companies the memory of this  fine should last just as long."

The Commission said the lifts installed in its own headquarters as well as at the European Court of Justice in Luxembourg were covered by the  cartel. It urged customers of the companies involved in the agreement to renegotiate their contracts, and said they might have the right to sue for damages.

John Pheasant, a competition law specialist and partner at law firm Hogan &Hartson, said yesterday's fines were part of a wider trend. "In terms of  fines, the Commission is now the toughest authority in the world. The trend towards higher  fines started a few years back, but recently there has been a distinct acceleration."

The Commission last year passed new fining guidelines that will allow Brussels to further increase the financial pain on abusive companies in all new cases. Officials have argued that the new rules will mean that more companies will be hit with the maximum penalty - a  fine equivalent to 10 per cent of their annual global turnover.

The lift  cartel operated in Germany, the Netherlands, Belgium and Luxembourg between at least 1995 and 2004. According to the  Commission, the groups co-ordinated their bids to ensure that a designated group would win specific contracts.

"(To agree on bids) theyusually met in bars and restaurants, they travelled to the countryside or even abroad, and they used pre-paid mobile cards to avoid tracking," the  Commission said.

Page 144 of 169 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
ThyssenKrupp, United Technologies,  Schindler and Kone said they were studying the Commission ruling, with  Schindler adding it was "very surprised" by the high  fines. United Technologies said Otis would appeal against the decision.

HD              EU imposes  record fine on lift cartels
WC             441 words
PD              22 February 2007
SN              Indo-Asian News Service
SC              HNIANS
LA               English

LP
Indo-Asian News Service Brussels, Feb 22 -- The European  Commission has handed down a
record antitrust  fine of almost one billion euros ($1 billion) to several lift companies for running illegal
cartels.

A combined penalty worth 992 million euros - the biggest ever  fine imposed by the European Union's antitrust watchdog for cartel violations - was imposed Wednesday on lift and  elevator makers Otis, Kone, Schindler and ThyssenKrupp groups.

TD
The companies were accused of operating cartels for the installation and maintenance of lifts and
escalators in Belgium, Germany, Luxembourg and the Netherlands between 1995 and 2004.

The European  Commission conducted surprise inspections in January 2004 at the premises of those companies throughout Europe after receiving a tip-off about the  cartel.

The three-year investigation revealed that they rigged bids for procurement contracts, fixed prices and allocated projects to each other, shared markets and exchanged commercially important and confidential information.
Even the European  Commission's headquarters in Brussels and the EU courts building in
Luxembourg were affected by their  cartel arrangement.

The effects of this  cartel may continue for 20-50 years as maintenance is often done by the companies that installed the equipment in the first place, and by cartelising the installation, the companies distorted the markets for years to come, the  commission said.

Among those companies, ThyssenKrupp alone had to pay almost half of the total  fine - the heaviest ever imposed by the commission on a single company in a cartel case. The German group's  fine was increased by 50 percent to 480 million euros because it was a repeated offender.

Otis, a unit of US conglomerate United Technologies Corp, received a  fine of 225 million euros, followed by Switzerland-based  Schindler with a penalty of 144 million euros and  Kone of Finland with 142 million euros.
Japanese company Mitsubishi was also  fined 1.8 million euros for its role limited to the Netherlands. "The damage caused by this  cartel will last for many years because it covered not only the initial
supply but also the subsequent maintenance of lifts and  elevators.  For these companies, the
memory of this  fine should last just as long," EU competition commissioner Neelie Kroes said.

The United Technologies Corp said in a statement that it felt disappointed with the  commission's decision and will appeal to the European Court of First Instance. Nine  Otis employees in Europe have since been identified and dismissed for breaking the company's code of ethics.
ThyssenKrupp also said it was considering appealing against the decision. Published by HT Syndication with permission from Indo-Asian News Service. HTS ir 070222-408308

HD           Lift firms are floored  by EC's €992m cartel fine
BY           Bernard Purcell
WC          351 words
PD           22 February 2007
SN           Irish Independent
SC           IINM
LA            English
CY           (c) 2007 Independent Newspapers Ireland Ltd

LP
BUILDING owners, property developers and hospital managers have been urged by Brussels to renegotiate their lift and  elevator maintenance contracts - or go to court - after a  cartel of major manufacturers was hit with the biggest anti-trust  fine in EU history.

The European  Commission yesterday fined lift manufacturers €992m for  price-fixing and carving up markets in Belgium, Germany, Luxembourg and the Netherlands.

TD
The rigged contracts, by the biggest names in the business, extended to the  Commission's own
Brussels HQ, the Berlaymont, and to the Court of Justice in Luxembourg.

Germany's ThyssenKrupp received the highest-ever penalty for a single company, €480m - double that of the next-nearest  fine because the company, described as "recidivist" by the  Commission, is a repeat offender.
The Commission fined Otis, owned by United Technologies of the United States, €225m.  Kone of
Finland was fined €142m. Schindler of Switzerland was  fined €144m.

Otis estimates that the equivalent of the entire world's population travels on its lifts,  escalators and moving walkways every 9 days. The companies fixed prices, rigged bids and shared out projects for lifts and  escalators in hospitals, railway stations, shopping centres, office blocks and commercial buildings to each other between 1995 and 2004.

Competition Commissioner Neelie Kroes said: "It is outrageous that the construction and maintenance costs of buildings, including hospitals, have been artificially bloated by these cartels. The national management of these companies knew what they were doing was wrong, but they tried to conceal
their action and went ahead anyway. The damage caused by this  cartel will last for many years because it covered not only the initial supply but also the subsequent maintenance of lifts and elevators."

Her spokesman, Jonathan Todd, who urged companies in all other member states to renegotiate their maintenance contracts or go to court, said the damage "will be felt for the next 20-50 years because a lot of these companies make their money from the maintenance which is done following the installation of the escalators and elevators".
Bernard Purcell

SE          Business
HD          EU fines of Euro 992m for lift firms step up war on cartels
BY          David Charter in Brussels
WC         377 words
PD          22 February 2007
SN          The Times
SC          T
LA           English
CY          © 2007 Times Newspapers Ltd. All rights reserved

LP
The penalty for being caught running a cartel in Europe increased dramatically yesterday when a group of lift companies was hit with record fines totalling Euro 992 million (Pounds 666 million).

Managers from the "big four" lift providers -Otis, Schindler, ThyssenKrupp and  Kone -were caught rigging contracts in four European countries.

TD
Deals to install and maintain  elevators  for hospitals, shopping centres and commercial buildings were carved up between the businesses, the European  Commission found.The companies even colluded over new lifts for the  Commission's headquarters in Brussels, as well as for the European Court of Justice in Luxembourg, officials said yesterday.

The fine beats a Euro 750 million penalty imposed last month on 11 firms in a  cartel for gas-insulated switchgear products. It showed that Neelie Kroes, the Competition Commissioner, meant business when she vowed last year to use more of power available to her. She can  fine a company up to 10 per cent of its turnover for  cartel activity.

Mrs Kroes said that managers of the lift companies met in bars away from main cities and, to avoid detection, even used pay-as-you-go mobile phones when deciding whose "turn" it was to win a contract.

She said: "National management of these companies knew what they were doing was wrong, but they tried to conceal their action and went ahead anyway. The damage caused by this  cartel will last for many years because it covered not only the initial supply, but also the subsequent maintenance of lifts and elevators.  For these companies, the memory of this  fine should last just as long."

ThyssenKrupp, branded a repeat offender because the German group was found to be in a steel  cartel in 1998, was given the largest antitrust  fine for one company so far, of about Euro 479 million. It said that it may fight its  fine's size, as did Finland's Kone, fined Euro 142 million.  Otis, part of the American UTC, was fined Euro 224 million and the Swiss Schindler Euro 143 million.
Misubishi Elevator Europe was fined Euro 1.8 million for its role in the Netherlands. (c) Times Newspapers Ltd, 2007

HD     Handelsblatt: Industry in turmoil as Thyssen-Krupp still under cartel investigations (Zoff in der
Aufzugbranche).
WC    215 words
PD     26 February 2007
SN     Handelsblatt
SC     HB
LA      English
CY     The Financial Times Limited. European Intelligence Wire. All material subject to copyright. Handelsblatt ©
2007 All rights reserved.

LP
The European Union's  cartel procedure filed against four leading lift and  escalator  manufacturers for holding illegal price agreements has left the industry in turmoil, with the companies reportedly accusing each other of holding agreements in order to come under principal witness regulations and to avoid record fines. After years of investigation, German steel and engineering company Thyssen-Krupp, Finnish rival Kone, US rival  Otis and Swiss rival Schindler Group were last week  fined 992m euros by the European Commission for fixing prices, manipulating tenders and exchanging confidential information.
Thyssen-Krupp has to pay the largest share at 479m euros, owing to the company's having been found guilty of illegal price agreements in the past.

TD
The investigations have resulted in a  cartel in Austria being uncovered, whereby Thyssen-Krupp has admitted to its first  cartel violation and submitted to principal witness regulations, which means that it may escape punishment. The  cartel court in Vienna is still investigating the matter; a representative for Thyssen-Krupp would not comment. The scandal in Austria undermines the arguments put forward by the manufacturers of the price agreements being a problem limited to small markets.
Abstracted from Handelsblatt

HD       Call for neutral body to probe cartel behavior
BY       Timothy Chui
WC      698 words
PD       26 February 2007
SN       The Standard
SC       HKIMAL
LA        English
CY       Copyright 2007 The Standard Newspapers Publishing Limited. All Rights Reserved.

LP
Hong Kong people will remain vulnerable to rip-offs and  price- fixing  until the government introduces a comprehensive competition law endowed with the powers of investigation, a senior Consumer Council official said.

``Many business people understand that cartels are unethical, but for us to be hostage to their moral judgment is unsatisfactory,'' Victor Hung Tin- yau, the council's chief officer of research and trade practices, said.

TD
``We need to be able to substantiate claims and to investigate cases along with the necessary powers to carry out these ends. The fact that we haven't allocated anyone with any investigative powers means we've left ourselves vulnerable.''
Hung made the remarks in wake of the European Union's antitrust  commission, which Wednesday
levied a record fine of nearly 1 billion euros (HK$10.22 billion) against five lift manufacturers, which all do
business in Hong Kong.

The judgment against  Otis, Schindler, ThyssenKrupp,  Kone and Mitsubishi arose from accusations they were engaged in an illegal cartel that rigged bids for procurement contracts, fixed prices, shared out projects and carved up markets in the installation and maintenance of lifts and  escalators in Belgium, Germany, Luxembourg and the Netherlands between 1995 and 2004. ThyssenKrupp was handed the heaviest fine of 480 million euros due to repeat offenses.

Among the five companies' numerous Hong Kong operations are The Bank of China Tower, Two International Finance Centre, the Hong Kong Police Headquarters, Hong Kong Convention and Exhibition Centre and the Mong Kok footbridge.

Legislator Ronny Tong Ka-wah said despite suspected  cartel behavior locally among supermarkets, property markets, fresh pork suppliers, ports and exhibition services, the lack of any independent and neutral body charged with adequate investigative powers to detect and substantiate claims would make enforcement of fair competition difficult, if not impossible.

He acknowledged external forces were also pressuring the territory to adopt mechanisms to protect fair competition as foreign chamber of commerce members were from nations with conventions of free competition and similar practices.

Although there exist few barriers to entry into Hong Kong markets, Tong believed existing business conduct that impeded competition was particularly worrisome for foreign investors who were new to the market.

Additionally, international institutions such as the EU and International Monetary Fund have been pressuring the Hong Kong government to adopt measures to protect competition within markets.

In its 2002 Trade Policy Review on Hong Kong, the World Trade Organization said: ``The seeming lack of coherent measures to address anti-competitive practices in all but a few sectors could constitute an obstacle to greater competition.''


Page 157 of 169 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
Commenting on the massive  fines levied by Competition Commissioner Neelie Kroes against a number of European companies, Tong believed there should be a cap commensurate with a company's turnover.

``The Civic Party wants to set a cap on  fines in relation to a business' turnover, say 20 to 30 percent. Fines can't be too small, otherwise enterprises would just factor them into their pricing strategies.  Fines are to ensure that laws are complied with. We are targeting a particular type of behavior, not a trader's position in a certain market,'' Tong said, adding that companies not engaged in unethical practices had nothing to worry about.

The Competition Policy Review Committee says a comprehensive law should specifically target anti- competitive practices such as  price-fixing, bid-rigging, market allocation, sales and production quotas, joint boycotts, and unfair or discriminatory standards, as well as abuse of a dominant market position.

The committee suggested such a law would not regulate ``natural'' monopolies or mergers and acquisitions.
It also proposed setting up a regulatory authority with sufficient investigative powers.

However, a critic of anti- competitive practices law, the Chinese Manufacturers' Association, believes the market place is the best arbitrator of resource allocation and not the courts.

``We fail to see how creating an uncertain, litigative and costly environment helps SMEs compete with the big guys, who are more experienced and better equipped to deal with just such an environment,'' it said.

The consultation paper on free competition is pending a report to determine the more concrete aspects of unfair competition.
Source : The Standard.

HD           UPDATE 2-Schindler shares fall as core unit disappoints
BY           By Katie Reid
WC          602 words
PD           26 February 2007
ET           09:33 AM
SN           Reuters News
SC           LBA
LA           English
CY           (c) 2007 Reuters Limited

LP
(Recasts, adds share price, analyst's comment, outlook)

ZURICH, Feb 26 (Reuters) - Shares in Swiss  elevator and escalator  maker Schindler plunged on Monday as investors shunned a 27-percent rise in full-year group net profit and focused on disappointing margins in its core unit.

TD
The group failed to reach its medium-term 10 percent operating profit margin (EBIT) target in its elevator and escalator  unit, posting a margin on an operating level of 8.4 percent excluding a real estate gain, as disproportionate growth of new installations weighed on margins.
Chief Financial Officer Erich Ammann told Reuters on the sidelines of a press conference that the
2007 EBIT margin in the core unit would be between 9 percent and 9.5 percent, as production
bottlenecks ease.
By 1036 GMT, participation certificates in  Schindler, its most widely traded form of equity, had fallen
7.1 percent to 79.55 Swiss francs per share, well behind a 0.4 percent rise in the DJ Stoxx European
industrial goods index <.SXNP>.

"Overall we judge these results as being in-line on first glance, but rather 5 percent below expectations when looking at the adjusted operating numbers," said Vontobel analyst Remo Rosenau in a note.

"The outlook is not highly encouraging and we will not increase our numbers, as they are already in line with the new company guidance," he said.

Schindler, which posted a group net profit of 511 million Swiss francs ($412 million) in 2006, said it expected to report a group net profit of around 530 million Swiss francs ($427.4 million) for 2007, excluding the EU  fine and any unforeseeable events.
The group said it was aiming for a group net profit of more than 600 million francs in 2008. Operating profit in the group's  elevators  and escalators unit rose to 717 million francs, from 632
million francs in the year ago period, but this included a gain before tax of 63 million francs from the
sale of real estate.
DISAPPOINTING

Schindler said it had secured orders for new installations for buildings such as the Federation Tower in Moscow, Europe's tallest building, but it makes more money by maintaining and servicing lifts than through new installations.

"These results are somewhat disappointing on the surface, and it looks as if the company has had difficulties working down such a abnormally large order increase," Helvea analyst Volkan Goecmen said in a note.



Page 159 of 169 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
The group, whose lifts and escalators move around 700 million people every day, competes with Germany's ThyssenKrupp , Otis, owned by United Technologies of the United States, and  Kone of Finland.

Analysts were disappointed that the operating margin-gap versus  Otis and Kone had increased, as both firms posted increases in their operating margins for the full-year.

The group said it would propose a dividend of 1.30 francs per share, versus 0.90 francs per share last year after the 1 to 10 share split.

It also appointed Juergen Tinggren as president of the core  elevator and escalator  unit, the senior position on the company's management committee.

Last week, Schindler said it would appeal against a European Commission decision to fine it 144 million euros for price fixing.  Schindler said it will record its fine in 2007.

The Commission fined Schindler, ThyssenKrupp,  Otis, Kone and Japan's Mitsubushi Electric a total of 992 million euros for fixing prices and carving up markets in the biggest  cartel penalty in European Union history.

HD             Kone to decide on EU fine appeal soon
WC           163 words

HELSINKI, Feb 26 (Reuters) - Head of the Finnish lift and escalator  maker Kone said on Monday the company will soon decide whether it is going to appeal the cartel fine imposed by the European Commission last week.

"We have not yet received the report -- when we receive it, we are ready to make decisions about follow-up actions on a rapid schedule," Chief Executive Matti Alahuhta told Reuters at the sidelines of a seminar.

TD
He added this would mean a week or two.

On Feb. 21, the European  Commission said it had fined Kone 142 million euros ($187 million) for anticompetitive practices in Germany and the Netherlands.

Swiss Schindler , fined 144 million euros in the same case, has announced that it will appeal the decision.
Other companies  fined were ThyssenKrupp of Germany,  Otis, owned by United Technologies of the
United States and Mitsubishi Electric of Japan.

Page 161 of 169 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.



SE     The World This Week
HD     COMMENT. WC    426 words
PD     26 February 2007
SN     ICIS Chemical Business
SC     ACHEN
LA     English
CY     Copyright 2007. Reed Business Information Limited. All rights reserved.

LP
Cartel fines reach new heights Cartels are in the news once again. Last week, the European Commission handed down its largest ever  fine - £992m ($1.3bn) - to four makers of lifts, or  elevators  to our transatlantic readers. The companies hit were  Otis, Kone, Schindler and ThyssenKrupp, who were judged to have operated cartels in four EU countries in the installation and maintenance of lifts and escalators. This exceeds the previous record fine of £790m, handed down in 2001 to companies involved in the widely reported and operated vitamins cartel. To its shame, the chemicals industry also holds the fourth, sixth and seventh positions on the fines league table, for cartels in synthetic rubber (2006), hydrogen peroxide (2006) and acrylic glass (2006), respectively. In the past four years, there have been fines levied on no fewer than 12 cartels in the chemicals sector, very nearly half of the total of 25  fines.

TD
By company, the worst offenders, as judged by the Commission's fines are: Roche, ENI, BASF, Arkema, Solvay and Shell. These occupy six of the top 10 cartel league places. Now, it has to be said that these are not usually thought of as the cowboys of the industry the fly-by-night operators. Indeed, all involved outwardly boast of their probity, their corporate responsibility and their stringent governance. So what is going wrong, time and time again? How many more cartels need to be uncovered? There are plenty in the pipeline still under investigation, and no doubt more that have not seen the light of day, despite the European Commission's leniency programme that offers immunity to whistleblowers that cooperate fully
in subsequent investigations. It is an issue that the chemical industry needs to come to grips with more effectively than it does at present. There is no excuse for inaction: the  fines are increasingly punitive the follow-up claims for damages from injured parties increasingly frequent, and, indeed, encouraged by the Commission and the effect on the public's perception of and trust in the industry is damaging. It should be the responsibility of line managers and management boards to put safeguards in place, and stress that illegal activity is neither condoned nor ignored. The pressure to improve prices and margins by
manipulating markets in concert is no excuse, and indeed in many cases is non-productive. Let's hope that we soon see an end to these anti- competitive practices.
Do you agree or disagree? Email the editor john.baker@icis.com

Commentaires d'analystes (texte original):

"JP Morgan" (Nick Paton): Following the margin disappointment in 1H 06, our estimates look for the company to miss its 10% margin target in the E&E division with a 9.8% margin in the 2H, after an 8.6% margin in the 1H. The companys focus on new equipment will, we believe, put downward pressure on the margin which investors are unlikely to give the company credit for in the short term. We believe  Schindler will seek a structural exit from its ALSO business but that it needs to get greater scale before doing so. We believe ALSO needs revenues of Sfr8bn, a significant increase on our 2006 estimate of Sfr2.9bn. We believe the company has to increase its exposure to this unappealing business before an exit. "Neutral"

"ZKB" (Martin Hüsler): Nebst den Zahlen 2006 richten wir unser Augenmerk auf den Ausblick für 2007 und auf die strategische Weiterentwicklung der Logistiktochter ALSO. 2006 dürfte wachstumsbedingt einen Margenrückgang gebracht haben, da das profitablere Servicegeschäft weniger dynamisch gewachsen ist. Die höhere Dynamik im Neuanlagegeschäft wird jedoch zunehmend auch Unterhaltsgeschäft nach sich ziehen und zukünftig zu Margenverbesserungen beitragen. "Übergewichten"

"Vontobel" (Serge Rotzer): We will be interested in learning about the progress made in H2 06 regarding the implementation of the new growth strategy. The motto in H1 06 was 'growth before margins', leading Schindler to post stronger organic growth than its main competitors (13% vs. 10% for  Kone and ~5% for Otis). The price to be paid for this strategy was a temporary fall in the margin from 9.2% to 8.6% -  Otis
and Kone were both able to increase their margins during the same period. We will also be keen to get an assessment from management concerning a possible  fine in connection with the ongoing EU
investigation. "Market Perform"

"Credit Suisse" (Maria Ivek): We are optimistic about the demand outlook for  Schindlers Elevator and Escalator  (E+E) operations, with service and modernisation forecast to remain strong in Europe owing, in particular, to the SNEL (Safety Norm for Existing Lifts) legislation for existing  elevator systems being implemented across Europe. Demand for new installations should also remain solid owing to the global launch last year of Schindlers innovative E+E product portfolio. [...]ALSOs expansion plans will not be financed by Schindler and we would consider a potential disposal of the 65% stake in ALSO in the medium term as a positive for  Schindler. "Neutral"

LP
Accounting schemes to cost software firm $30M
BREACH OF CONTRACT

TD
CLEVELAND (AP)—Banking and financial services firm KeyCorp will receive $279 million from a Swiss insurance company to settle a six-year-old suit over losses KeyCorp claimed in its car-leasing business.

The settlement resolves a lawsuit KeyCorp filed against New York-based Swiss Reinsurance America Corp., part of Zurich-based Swiss Reinsurance Corp. The issue was whether Swiss Reinsurance was obligated to cover the losses stemming from the lease of about 160,000 cars between 1997 and 2000 by KeyCorp. When leased cars are returned and the company is unable to sell them for fair-market value, the difference is considered a loss and is insured.

WASHINGTON (AP)— Waste Management Inc. has been ordered to pay about $29 million dollars to settle a dispute involving its sale of a joint venture in Mexico.

Waste Management sold its interest in a joint venture in Mexico in 2000. The purchaser, which wasn't identified, sued Waste Management over the value of the joint venture. The payment includes monetary damages, interest dating back to 2000, and certain fees and expenses.
INTELLECTUAL PROPERTY

SEATTLE (AP)— Microsoft Corp. must pay $1.52 billion in damages to telecommunications equipment maker Alcatel-Lucent S.A. for violating two patents related to digital music, a San Diego jury ruled last week.

Microsoft said the patents govern the conversion of audio into the digital MP3 file format on personal computers. In 2003, Lucent Technologies Inc., which last year was acquired by Alcatel, filed 15 patent claims against Gateway Inc. and Dell Inc. for technology developed by Bell Labs, its research arm. In April
2003, Microsoft added itself to the list of defendants, saying the patents were closely tied to its Windows operating system.
Microsoft disputed that Paris-based Alcatel-Lucent's patents govern its MP3 tools, and said it licenses the
MP3 software used by its Windows Media Player from a German company.
NEGLIGENCE

BIRMINGHAM, ALA. (AP)—An Alabama state jury found Brookwood Medical Center at fault for the suicide of a psychiatric patient who escaped custody and jumped to his death from a hospital parking deck. The jury ordered the hospital to pay $12 million in punitive damages.

Vaughn Hollon was 53 in October 2003 when he was admitted to the hospital as a suicide risk. He died two days after he scaled a fence in an outdoor area of a psychiatric ward. He broke both legs after an initial fall to a parking deck, and was killed by a second jump that sent him plunging 80 feet to the ground.
PRODUCTS LIABILITY


Page 166 of 169 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
LOS ANGELES (AP)—A California federal judge has approved a $13.55 million settlement of a lawsuit filed against Boeing Co. and three other companies by two soldiers who were hurt in a 2003 Army helicopter crash in Iraq.

The products liability lawsuit claimed that a gear box on an AH-64 Apache Longbow failed during a maintenance flight in Tikrit, causing the chopper to plunge 800 feet. Former Chief Warrant Officer Juan Beltran, 34, was left a quadriplegic. The crash shattered the spine of Chief Warrant Officer Ron Carnes,
34. He can walk but his movement is restricted.

The lawsuit claimed that the gearbox and gearbox bearings were inadequately lubricated and that a device called an accelerometer that should have warned pilots of a problem was badly designed and had failed early.
REGULATORY ACTION

BRUSSELS (AP)—European Union regulators have  finedUnited Technologies Corp.'s Otis unit and four other elevator makers $1.3 billion for operating cartels while installing and maintaining elevators  and escalators in Germany, Belgium, Luxembourg and the Netherlands.

Also fined were German conglomerate ThyssenKrupp A.G.; Finland's Kone Corp.; Switzerland's Schindler Holding A.G.; and subsidiaries of Japan's Mitsubishi Elevator Europe B.V. The European Commission said the cartel had worked to rig bids for procurement contracts, fix prices and share markets "between at least 1995 and 2004."

E.U. regulators found evidence from "numerous documents and corporate statements" to prove the companies allocated tenders and contracts for the sale, installation and maintenance of  elevators  "with the aim of freezing market shares and fixing prices," the  commission said.

SAN FRANCISCO (AP)—Securities regulators have  fined software maker Veritas Software Corp. $30 million to settle accusations that the company engaged in various accounting schemes in the years leading to its sale to Symantec in 2005.

Regulators opened an inquiry into a deal Veritas negotiated with AOL in 2000.That arrangement, involving a quid pro quo purchase of software licenses and advertising, improperly increased the revenue of each company by $20 million. Veritas then lied to auditors about the deal and helped AOL conceal its involvement, according to the U.S. Securities and Exchange  Commission.

The probe also found that Veritas manipulated its books in 2001, 2002 and 2003 in an effort to produce results that would please shareholders.
